The Five Linguistic Roots of Munazara and Their Ethical Implications
Linguistic derivation of munazara and ethical implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five linguistic roots from which munazara is derived and explain the ethical conduct each root implies for the disputants.

Core Idea
Munazara's linguistic roots teach that debate requires similarity, reflection, patience, and confrontation.

The Rashidiyya commentary begins its definition of munazara by exploring its linguistic origins. The author lists five possible derivations, each pointing to a specific ethical norm for debate. Understanding these roots reveals that Islamic dialectical tradition is not merely about winning arguments but about principled conduct. In this lesson, we will examine each root and its implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five roots
List the five derivations given in the passage: from nazir (counterpart), nazar (sight), attention to intelligibles, waiting, and confrontation.
The order in the text is: nazir, nazar (sight), attention/intelligibles, waiting, confrontation.
2
Analyze the implication of 'nazir'
The root 'counterpart' implies that the two debaters should be similar in status, neither too high nor too low. This ensures a balanced exchange.
3
Analyze the implication of 'attention to intelligibles'
The third root implies the priority of reflection: one should not speak without prior reflection on what one intends to say.
The second root (sight) is not given an explicit implication in the passage; it is simply listed.
4
Analyze the implication of 'waiting'
The fourth root implies that it is fitting for one disputant to wait until the other completes his speech, not interrupt.
5
Integrate the insights
These linguistic derivations collectively shape the ideal character of a munazar: mutual respect, thoughtfulness, patience, and direct engagement.
The fifth root (confrontation) is listed but not elaborated; it suggests a direct, face-to-face encounter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة ففي التعريفات ( أي أما المفهوم الكلي الذي هو مقدمة مذكورة في هذه الرسالة فهي منحصرة في التعريفات وما يتعلق بها، والمقدمة مأخوذة من مقدمة الجيش، ووجه المناسبة غير خفي على أحد من المحصلين . والتعريفات جمع تعريف بمعنى المعرف، أو على معناه المصدري أعني الفكر والنظر لتحصيل تصور .

ولما كانت المناظرة هي المقصودة بالنظر ههنا قدمها وبدأ بتعريفها فقال المناظرة مأخوذة إما من النظير بمعنى أن مأخذهما شيء واحد أو من النظر بمعنى الإبصار ، أو بمعنى التفات النفس إلى المعقولات والتأمل فيها ، أو بمعنى الانتظار أو بمعنى المقابلة . ووجه المناسبة غير خفي . وفي الأول إيحاء إلى أنه ينبغي أن يكون المناظران متماثلين، بأن لا يكون أحدهما في غاية العلو والكمال ، والآخر في نهاية الدناءة والنقصان ، وفي الثالث إيحاء إلى أولوية التأمل، بأن لا يقول ما لم يتأمل فيما يريد أن يقول، وفي الرابع إلى أنه جدير أن ينتظر أحد المتخاصمين إلى أن يتم كلام الآخر ، لا أن يتكلم في حال كلامه .

وفي الاصطلاح يقال لما يقول بقوله : ( توجه المتخاصمين في النسبة بين الشيئين إظهارا للصواب ) يريد قدس سره أن المتخاصمين أي اللذين مطلب أحدهما غير مطلب الآخر، إذا توجها في النسبة بين الشيئين اللذين أحدهما محكوم عليه ، والآخر محكوم به ، وإن كان ذلك التوجه في النفس، كما كان للحكماء الإشراقيين وكان غرضهما من ذلك إظهار الحق والصواب ، يسمى ذلك التوجه مناظرة في الاصطلاح.
As for the introduction, it is concerning definitions (i.e., the universal concept that is an introduction mentioned in this treatise is confined to definitions and what pertains to them, and the introduction is taken from the introduction of the army, and the suitability is not hidden from any of the researchers. And definitions (al-ta'rifat) is the plural of definition (ta'rif) meaning the definiens, or on its verbal noun meaning, i.e., thought and reflection to acquire a concept).

And since debate (al-munazara) is the intended purpose of consideration here, he presented it and began by defining it, saying: munazara is derived either from al-nazir (the counterpart) meaning that their source is one thing, or from al-nazar (sight) meaning sight, or meaning the soul's attention to intelligibles and reflection upon them, or meaning waiting, or meaning confrontation. And the suitability is not hidden. The first implies that the two debaters should be similar, such that one of them is not at the utmost height and perfection while the other is at the extreme of lowliness and deficiency. The third implies the priority of reflection, such that one does not say what one has not reflected upon concerning what one intends to say. The fourth implies that it is fitting that one of the two disputants wait until the other completes his speech, not speak while he is speaking.

And technically, (the author) said by his statement: 'The orientation of the two disputants in the relation between two things to manifest what is correct.' He (may his secret be sanctified) intended that the two disputants, i.e., those whose demand of one is different from the demand of the other, when they orient toward the relation between the two things, one of which is the subject of the judgment and the other the predicate, even if that orientation is in the soul, as was the case with the Illuminationist philosophers, and their purpose in that is to manifest truth and what is correct, that orientation is called technically munazara.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from the Rashidiyya commentary defines the term 'munazara' (dialectical debate) in both linguistic and technical senses. The commentator first explains the structure of the treatise, noting that the introduction deals with definitions. He then explains that 'munazara' is derived from five possible roots: (1) from 'nazir' (counterpart), implying that both debaters are similar in status, (2) from 'nazar' (sight), (3) from 'nazar' meaning the soul's attention to intelligibles, (4) from 'waiting', and (5) from 'confrontation'. For each, he mentions the implication for the conduct of debate: from 'nazir' the need for similarity, from 'attention' the need for reflection, from 'waiting' the virtue of patience, and so on. Then he gives the technical definition as stated in the original matn: 'The orientation of the two disputants in the relation between two things to manifest what is correct.' He elaborates that the two disputants have different demands, and they direct their attention to the relationship between the subject and predicate of the judgment, even if only mentally, as did the Illuminationist philosophers. Their goal is to make truth manifest. This technical definition specifies the purpose (i.e., manifesting truth) and distinguishes munazara from mere argumentation. The commentary adheres to the typical pattern of explaining the linguistic roots to highlight important ethical aspects of debate.

Key Terms
نظير (nazir)
نظر (nazar)
التفات النفس إلى المعقولات
انتظار (intizar)
مقابلة (muqabala)
مناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which linguistic root of munazara implies that one debater should wait for the other to finish speaking?
A) النظير (al-nazir)
B) الإبصار (al-ibsar)
C) الانتظار (al-intizar)
D) المقابلة (al-muqabala)

Key Takeaway
The linguistic roots of munazara prescribe that debaters be equal, thoughtful, patient, and confrontational, not adversarial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Choose a term from Islamic theology or fiqh (e.g., 'kalam' meaning speech/theology, or 'ijtihad'). Research its linguistic roots. For each root, write a short explanation of how it informs the technical meaning of the term. Use the same method as the commentator used for munazara.

The Technical Definition of Munazara: Disputants Orienting Toward Truth
Technical definition of munazara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the technical definition of munazara into its four components: disputants, orientation, relation, and purpose.

Core Idea
Munazara is defined as disputants' orientation in the relation between two things to manifest truth.

After the linguistic derivations, the commentator presents the technical definition of munazara as given by the original author: 'the orientation of the two disputants in the relation between two things to manifest truth.' This definition is precise and distinguishes munazara from mere arguing. In this lesson, we will break down each component to understand what makes a dialectical exchange a valid munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the definition
The definition starts with 'the two disputants' (al-mutakhasimayn). These are the participants in munazara. The commentator clarifies that each has a different demand (matalib).
2
Identify the action
The disputants 'orient' (tawajjuh) themselves. This orientation can be mental or verbal, as even the Illuminationist philosophers did internally.
The passage mentions that orientation can be in the soul (fi al-nafs).
3
Identify the object of orientation
The orientation is 'in the relation between two things' (fi al-nisba bayn al-shay'ayn). One thing is the subject of judgment (mahkum 'alayh) and the other the predicate (mahkum bih).
4
Identify the purpose
The purpose is 'to manifest truth' (izharan li-l-sawab). The goal is not victory but the disclosure of what is correct.
The commentator emphasizes this distinguishes munazara from sophistry.
5
Distinguish munazara from other debates
Compare to mere argumentation: the defining feature is the truth-seeking aim. Also, note the condition that the two disputants have distinct demands – they are not agreeing from the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقدمة ففي التعريفات ( أي أما المفهوم الكلي الذي هو مقدمة مذكورة في هذه الرسالة فهي منحصرة في التعريفات وما يتعلق بها، والمقدمة مأخوذة من مقدمة الجيش، ووجه المناسبة غير خفي على أحد من المحصلين . والتعريفات جمع تعريف بمعنى المعرف، أو على معناه المصدري أعني الفكر والنظر لتحصيل تصور .

ولما كانت المناظرة هي المقصودة بالنظر ههنا قدمها وبدأ بتعريفها فقال المناظرة مأخوذة إما من النظير بمعنى أن مأخذهما شيء واحد أو من النظر بمعنى الإبصار ، أو بمعنى التفات النفس إلى المعقولات والتأمل فيها ، أو بمعنى الانتظار أو بمعنى المقابلة . ووجه المناسبة غير خفي . وفي الأول إيحاء إلى أنه ينبغي أن يكون المناظران متماثلين، بأن لا يكون أحدهما في غاية العلو والكمال ، والآخر في نهاية الدناءة والنقصان ، وفي الثالث إيحاء إلى أولوية التأمل، بأن لا يقول ما لم يتأمل فيما يريد أن يقول، وفي الرابع إلى أنه جدير أن ينتظر أحد المتخاصمين إلى أن يتم كلام الآخر ، لا أن يتكلم في حال كلامه .

As for the introduction, it is concerning definitions (i.e., the universal concept that is an introduction mentioned in this treatise is confined to definitions and what pertains to them, and the introduction is taken from the introduction of the army, and the suitability is not hidden from any of the researchers. And definitions (al-ta'rifat) is the plural of definition (ta'rif) meaning the definiens, or on its verbal noun meaning, i.e., thought and reflection to acquire a concept).

And the suitability is not hidden. The first implies that the two debaters should be similar, such that one of them is not at the utmost height and perfection while the other is at the extreme of lowliness and deficiency. The third implies the priority of reflection, such that one does not say what one has not reflected upon concerning what one intends to say. The fourth implies that it is fitting that one of the two disputants wait until the other completes his speech, not speak while he is speaking.

Understanding Mujadala and Mukabara: The Degenerate Forms of Debate
Author defines mujadala and mukabara as types of munazara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mujadala and mukabara from proper munazara by their definitions and purposes.

Core Idea
Mujadala and mukabara are debates without truth.

In Islamic logic, not all debates are virtuous. The author defines two types of contentious exchange that lack the proper goal of showing truth. Understanding these helps you identify when a debate is actually a sophistical exercise. This lesson unpacks the definitions and the subtle linguistic cues that signal these degenerate forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Both mujadala and mukabara are defined as types of munazara (contentious exchange). Recognize that munazara is the broader category.
The author uses 'al-munaza'a' as the genus.
2
Extract the differentia for mujadala
Mujadala's purpose is to compel the opponent, not to show truth. Note the contrast in goals between questioner and respondent.
If the disputant is a questioner, he aims to compel; if a respondent, he aims to avoid being compelled.
3
Extract the differentia for mukabara
Mukabara is stubborn opposition without any aim at truth. It is pure obstinacy.
The author only gives a brief definition, indicating it lacks even the minimal structure of mujadala.
4
Analyze the linguistic form
The term 'mujadala' uses a form indicating mutual participation (mufa'ala), even when only one side is disputing. The author explains this is because the disputant dominates and the other ignores.
This shows classical concern with how language reflects logical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأول : ما فسره بقوله ( والمجادلة هي المنازعة ؛ لا لإظهار الصواب ، بل لإلزام الخصم ، فإن كان المجادل مجيبا كان سعيه أن لا يلزم ويسلم عن إلزام الغير إياه ، وإن كان سائلا كان سعيه أن يلزم الغير. وقد يكون السائل والمجيب كلاهما مجادلين ، فلذا قال قدس سره : هي المنازعة التي تدل على المشاركة، وأما إذا كان المجادل أحدهما ، فلما كان شأن من غير المجادل أن لا يتوجه إلى قول المجادل ويعرض عنه ، غلب المجادل وأطلق صيغة المشاركة . والثاني : ما بينه بقوله ( والمكابرة هذه أي المنازعة لا لإظهار الصواب
The first: what he explained by his saying 'And disputation (al-mujādala) is contentious exchange (al-munāzaʿa); not for showing the truth, but for compelling the opponent. For if the disputant is a respondent, his effort is that he not be compelled and be safe from being compelled by the other, and if he is a questioner, his effort is to compel the other. And it may be that both the questioner and the respondent are disputants; therefore he (may Allah sanctify his secret) said: it is contentious exchange that indicates participation. And if only one of them is a disputant, then because the nature of the non-disputant is that he does not turn to the statement of the disputant and turns away from it, the disputant predominates and the form of participation is used. And the second: what he explained by his saying 'And stubbornness (al-mukābara) is this, i.e., contentious exchange not for showing the truth'.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines two subtypes of contentious exchange that fall outside proper debate. The first is mujādala (disputation), where the goal is not to show the truth but to force the opponent into submission. The commentator explains that if the disputant is a respondent, he tries to avoid being compelled; if he is a questioner, he tries to compel. When both parties are disputants, the term 'mujādala' implies mutual participation, so the verbal form indicates sharing. When only one is a disputant, the other ignores him, and the form of participation is still used because the disputant dominates. The second type is mukābara (stubbornness), which is contentious exchange that does not aim at truth at all, but rather relies on sheer obstinacy. The passage thus distinguishes proper debate (munāẓara) from its degenerate forms.

Key Terms
المجادلة
المكابرة
المنازعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of mujadala?
A) It aims to show the truth to both parties
B) It seeks to compel the opponent without aiming at truth
C) It is a cooperative form of inquiry
D) It is always conducted by a single person

Key Takeaway
Mujadala and mukabara are distinguished from proper debate by their lack of intention to show truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a transcript of a debate on the creation of the Qur'an, identify which parts are mujadala (intent to compel) and which are proper munazara (intent to show truth).
?
Write a brief dialogue where one speaker uses mukabara (stubborn denial) and explain the logical flaw.

Naql: Conditions for Honest Transmission
Commentary defines naql and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for honest transmission (naql) and apply them to evaluate citations in Islamic scholarly debates.

Core Idea
Naql is citation with meaning preserved and attribution explicit—it's the scholar's trustworthiness in transmission.

This passage defines 'naql' (transmission) as a cornerstone of scholarly debate. It explains that transmitting another's statement requires faithfulness to meaning, not exact words, and clear attribution. Understanding naql is essential for distinguishing proper citation from misrepresentation in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of naql
Find the sentence starting with 'والنَّقْل : هُوَ الْإِتْيَانُ بِقَوْلِ الْغَيْرِ' in the Arabic. Identify the core components: bringing another's statement, as it is in meaning, showing it is from another.
2
Extract the two conditions
In the footnote, the text lists two conditions for honesty in naql: (1) meaning not changed, even if wording changed; (2) showing it is the statement of another. Write them down.
3
Apply to an example
The passage gives an example: 'Abu Hanifa said: Intention in ablution is not obligatory.' Check that meaning is preserved and attribution is explicit. If someone changed the wording but kept the meaning and said 'According to Abu Hanifa, intention is not obligatory,' that would be valid naql. If they omitted the attribution, it would be dishonest.
4
Distinguish from other uses
Note that naql here is not for refutation or truth-seeking per se; it is simply citation. In usul al-fiqh, naql can mean transmission of hadith; here it is a dialectical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنَّقْل : هُوَ الْإِتْيَانُ بِقَوْلِ الْغَيْرِ عَلَى مَا هُوَ عَلَيْهِ بِحَسَبِ المَعْنَى مُظْهِرًا أَنَّهُ قَولُ الْغَيْرِ . إلا أنه لا لإلزام الخصم أيضًا ، كما أنه ليس لإظهار الصواب) . وتذكير الضمير في أنه ، لأن المصدر ذا التاء يذكر ويؤنث . ثم لما فرغ من تعريف المناظرة وضديها اللذين بهما تبين حقيقتها - كما قال المحققون : حقائق الأشياء تتبين بأضدادها - وكان النقل من الكتاب ، أو من الثقة في زماننا أولى من الإثبات بالدليل، لكونه مُفْضيًا إلى كثرة النزاع أردفه بتعريفه فقال : ( والعقل هو الإتيان بقول الغير على ما هو عليه بحسب المعنى ، مظهرًا أنه قول الغير ) يريد أنه لا يلزم في النقل الإتيان بقول الغير، بحيث لا يتغير لفظه ، بل إنما يلزم الإتيان به على وجه لا يتغير معناه ، ومع ذلك يلزم إظهار أنه قول الغير ) ، كأن يقول مثلا : قال أبو حنيفة - رحمه الله تعالى - : النية في الوضوء ليست بفرض، وأما الإتيان بقول الغير، على وجه لا يظهر منه أنه قول الغير - لا صريحا (۱) فأنواع المخاصمة الكلامية ثلاثة ؛ لأنه إما أن يكون الغرض منها إظهار الصواب ، وهذه تسمى ( مناظرة ) ، وإما أن يكون الغرض منها إلزام الخصم ، لا إظهار الصواب وهي (المجادلة) ، وإما أن يكون الغرض منها المخاصمة فقط ، لا إلزام الخصم ، ولا إظهار الصواب وهي (المكابرة) . ولا تكون الأولى إلا ممن تشبعت نفوسهم بحب الحق، وأما الأخريان فإنهما يكثران عند انتشار الجهل وحب الغلية ولو بالباطل. وإن هذا كثير في زماننا ، فهو يشبه كثيرا العصر الذي ظهر فيه السوفسطائيون في اليونان في القرن الخامس قبل الميلاد (۲) هذا تمهيد لتعريف النقل. (۳) فيشترط في أمانة النقل أمران : أولهما أن لا يغير المعنى ، ولو غير اللفظ ، وثانيهما أن يظهر أنه قول الغير . وهذا من الصفات الواجبة للعلماء ، فلا يكون العالم مشوها للحقائق، بل ينبغي أن يكون أمنيا ، فلا يدعي لنفسه ما ليس له . ولا يكون هذا أيضا إلا إذا عم العلم وانتشر وأغرم الناس بحب الحق ، أما إذا كان الأمر على خلاف هذا فإن الأمانة تخفي وتضيع حتى بين علمائهم .
And transmission (naql) is bringing the statement of another as it is, in terms of meaning, showing that it is the statement of another. Except that it is not for refuting the opponent, nor for showing truth. And the masculine pronoun in 'that it' (annahu) is used because the masdar with the feminine ending can be masculine or feminine. Then, when he finished defining munazara and its two opposites through which its reality becomes clear—as the verifiers said: the realities of things become clear through their opposites—and since transmission from a book or from a trustworthy person in our time is better than proof by evidence, because it leads to much dispute, he followed it with its definition, saying: 'And transmission is the bringing of another's statement as it is in terms of meaning, showing that it is the statement of another.' He means that in transmission it is not necessary to bring the statement of another without changing its wording; rather, it is only necessary to bring it in a way that does not change its meaning, and with that it is necessary to show that it is the statement of another—like for example: Abu Hanifa—may God have mercy on him—said: 'Intention in ablution is not obligatory.' As for bringing the statement of another in a way that does not show that it is the statement of another—not explicitly. (1) The types of verbal contention are three: either its purpose is showing truth—this is called 'munazara'; or its purpose is refuting the opponent, not showing truth—that is 'jadal'; or its purpose is just contention, not refuting the opponent nor showing truth—that is 'mukabara'. The first is only from those whose souls are saturated with love of truth; the other two are frequent when ignorance spreads and love of domination even by falsehood prevails. This is abundant in our time, and it much resembles the age in which the Sophists appeared in Greece in the fifth century BC. (2) This is an introduction to the definition of transmission. (3) Two conditions are required in the honesty of transmission: first, that the meaning is not changed, even if the wording is changed; second, that it is shown to be the statement of another. This is among the obligatory traits for scholars, so that the scholar does not distort truths; rather, he should be trustworthy, not claiming for himself what is not his. This also only happens when knowledge is widespread and people are in love with truth; but if the situation is opposite, trustworthiness becomes hidden and is lost even among their scholars.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining 'naql' (transmission) and its conditions. The commentator first gives the definition: transmission is conveying another's statement while preserving the meaning and explicitly attributing it to the original source. He notes that transmission is not for refutation or truth-seeking per se, but is a method of citing authority. He explains that the definition uses a masculine pronoun for 'annahu' because the word 'naql' can be treated as masculine despite its feminine ending. The commentator then explains why the author placed the definition of naql after defining munazara and its opposites: because things are known by their opposites, and transmission is more reliable than argumentation in an age of dispute. He clarifies that preserving the meaning is essential; exact wording is not required. He gives an example: 'Abu Hanifa said: Intention in ablution is not obligatory.' The commentary includes two footnotes: one summarizing the three types of verbal contention (munazara, jadal, mukabara) with an observation that the first is rare and only for truth lovers, while the others dominate in times of ignorance—comparing it to the Sophists in Greece. The second footnote states the two conditions for honest transmission: (1) preserving meaning, even if wording changes, and (2) clearly attributing to the original source. These are necessary scholarly virtues.

Key Terms
النقل
المناظرة
المخاصمة الكلامية

A) Exact wording and explicit source attribution.

Key Takeaway
Honest naql requires preserving the original meaning and explicitly attributing the statement to its source, even if the wording changes.

Definitions of Tasḥīḥ al-Naql and al-Muddaʿī
Author defines two key terms for dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tasḥīḥ al-naql and al-muddaʿī in the context of Islamic dialectic and distinguish them from related terms.

Core Idea
Verification of transmission checks attribution, not truth; the claimant is the one who proves.

In this passage, the author lays down the foundational definitions for dialectical practice. Understanding these two terms precisely is essential before engaging in any disputation, because they determine who is responsible for what.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being defined
Look in the Arabic for the terms 'تصحيح النقل' and 'المدعي'. Notice that each is followed by a definition.
The terms are introduced at the beginning of the passage.
2
Analyse the definition of tasḥīḥ al-naql
The definition says it is 'بيان صدق نسبة ما نسب إلى المنقول عنه' (a statement of the truth of attribution). Focus on 'صدق النسبة' (truth of attribution) not truth of the statement itself.
This is a crucial distinction often misunderstood.
3
Analyse the definition of al-muddaʿī
The claimant is 'من نصب نفسه لإثبات الحكم بالدليل أو التنبيه' (the one who sets himself to establish the judgment by evidence or indication). Note the methods: dalīl or tanbīh.
In dialectic, the claimant is not just a plaintiff but the proponent of a claim.
4
Compare with everyday usage
How does this dialectical meaning differ from fiqh or general usage? In fiqh, muddaʿī is the plaintiff; here it is broader. Tasḥīḥ al-naql is specific to verifying attribution.
This comparison helps avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصحيح النقل: هو بيان صدق نسبة ما نسب إلى المنقول عنه والمُدَّعِي: من نصب نفسه لإثبات الحكم بالدليل أو التنبيه. ولا ضمنا ولا كناية ولا إشارة - فهو اقتباس والمقتبس مدع في اصطلاحهم. ثم اعلم أنه بعد ما نقل أحد المتخاصمين قولا - إن كانت صحته وكونه مطابقا للواقع معلومة للآخر - فلا يصح طلب تصحيحه فإنه - مع العلم بذلك - لو - طلب تصحيحه كان مكابرا أو مجادلا، وإن لم تكن له معلومة لابد له من طلب التصحيح، وإلا لم يكن مناظرا).
Verification of transmission: it is a statement of the truth of the attribution of what was attributed to the one from whom it is transmitted. And the claimant: the one who sets himself to establish the judgment by evidence or by indication. And not by implication, nor by metaphor, nor by allusion - so it is quotation, and the one quoted is a claimant in their terminology. Then know that after one of the two disputants transmits a statement - if its correctness and correspondence to reality is known to the other - then it is not permissible to demand its verification, for with knowledge of that, if he demanded its verification, he would be an obstinate person or a disputant. And if it is not known to him, he must demand verification, otherwise he would not be a disputant.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms of dialectic: 'taghih al-naql' (verification of transmission) and 'al-mudda'i' (the claimant). The author first gives a compact definition: taghih al-naql is showing that the attributed statement truly originates from the source, and al-mudda'i is the person who undertakes to prove a ruling by evidence or by direct indication. Then the author explains an important procedural point: when one disputant transmits a statement from an authority, the other disputant should not ask for verification if he already knows the statement is correct. Doing so would be obstinate or contentious. However, if he does not know its correctness, he must ask for verification; otherwise he would not be engaging in proper disputation. This rule ensures that the dialectical process focuses on genuine unknowns, not on wasting time over known facts.

Key Terms
تصحيح النقل
المدعي
مكابر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' consist of?
A) Proving that the transmitted statement is true in itself.
B) Showing that the attribution of the statement to its source is correct.
C) Verifying the reliability of the transmitter.
D) Establishing the judgment by evidence.

Key Takeaway
Tasḥīḥ al-naql is about correct attribution; al-muddaʿī is the one who proves a claim.

?

The Rule for Demanding Verification
Author states the rule for when to demand verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for when it is permissible or obligatory to demand verification of a transmitted statement in a dialectical setting.

Core Idea
Do not demand verification of what you already know; demand what you do not know.

After defining the terms, the author proceeds to an essential procedural rule. This rule distinguishes proper disputation from obstinacy. Understanding it prevents dialectical debates from devolving into pointless challenges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage divides the situation into two cases: when the correctness (ṣiḥḥa) and correspondence to reality (muṭābaqa li-l-wāqiʿ) of the transmitted statement are known to the other disputant, and when they are not known.
The condition is about knowledge of the statement's truth, not just attribution.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
If known, 'لا يصح طلب تصحيحه' (it is not permissible to demand verification). If unknown, 'لابد له من طلب التصحيح' (he must demand verification).
The ruling is categorical: forbidden when known, obligatory when unknown.
3
Identify the consequences of violating the rule
If one demands verification when it is known, he becomes 'مكابرا أو مجادلا' (obstinate or contentious). If one fails to demand when unknown, he is not a 'مناظرا' (proper disputant).
These consequences show the seriousness of adhering to the rule.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Think of a debate: if one disputant quotes a well-known verse, the opponent knows it is correct and should not demand verification. If the opponent doubts the quote's authenticity, he must ask.
Practice with examples from Islamic sciences.

Source Text

The Preference for 'Tasḥīḥ al-Naql' over 'Ṣiḥḥat al-Naql'
Author explains why 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' is preferred over 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' and 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' and explain why the former is preferred in the dialectical tradition.

Core Idea
Verification of transmission is about proving attribution, not about the truth of the content.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the definition of 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' (verification of transmission) by contrasting it with the similar-sounding term 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' (correctness of transmission). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the concept of transmission verification in dialectic and hadith criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'tasḥīḥ al-naql'
The commentator defines it as 'بيان صدق نسبة ما نسب إلى المنقول عنه' – a statement that the attribution of what was attributed to the source is true. Focus on the terms: 'tasḥīḥ' (verification) and 'naql' (transmission).
2
Identify the key difference between the two terms
Contrast 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' with 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql'. The latter would mean the transmission itself is correct (i.e., the content is true), while the former is about verifying the attribution, regardless of content truth.
3
Understand why the commentator prefers 'tasḥīḥ'
The commentator argues that what is sought is not the correctness of the transmission, but the verification that the transmitter correctly attributed the statement to the source. 'Tasḥīḥ al-naql' precisely captures this.
4
Note the stylistic choice of omitting conjunction
The commentator remarks that 'tasḥīḥ' is not conjoined with 'naql' because verification is a natural consequence of transmission, not a separate act.
This highlights the organic relationship between the two concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذا أردف قدس سره تعريف النقل بتعريف التصحيح فقال: تصحيح النقل هو بيان صدق نسبة ما نسب إلى المنقول عنه. وقوله تصحيح النقل أولى من قول القاضي العضد: صحة النقل لأن الظاهر منه كون النقل صحيحا، ولا يطلب ذلك، بل يطلب التصحيح، وهو إظهار أن ما نسب الناقل إلى المنقول عنه منسوب إليه في نفس الأمر، فافهم. وترك العطف لأن التصحيح من متعلقات النقل.
Therefore, the author (may his secret be sanctified) followed the definition of transmission with the definition of verification, and said: 'Verification of transmission is a statement of the truth of the attribution of what was attributed to the one from whom it is transmitted.' And his saying 'verification of transmission' is more appropriate than the saying of al-Qadi al-Abd: 'correctness of transmission', because the apparent meaning of that is that the transmission itself is correct, but that is not what is sought; rather what is sought is verification, which is to show that what the transmitter attributed to the source is indeed attributed to it in reality. So understand. And he did not use conjunction because verification is a consequence of transmission.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of 'tasḥīḥ al-naql'. The author explains why the commentator (may Allah sanctify his secret) chose to present the definition of verification after the definition of transmission. The commentator defines 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' as showing that the attribution of a statement to its source is truthful. He then argues that this expression 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' is better than al-Qadi al-Abd's expression 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' (correctness of transmission). The reason is that 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' suggests the transmission itself is correct, which is not the goal; rather, the goal is to verify the attribution—to demonstrate that the transmitter has correctly attributed the statement to its source. The author also notes that the term 'tasḥīḥ' is not coordinated with 'naql' because verification is an integral part of the process of transmission.

Key Terms
تصحيح النقل
صحة النقل

D) Because al-Qadi al-Abd used 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' and the commentator disagrees with him.

Key Takeaway
The term 'tasḥīḥ al-naql' precisely denotes the act of verifying attribution, whereas 'ṣiḥḥat al-naql' misleadingly implies the transmission is correct in content.

The Refined Definition of the Claimant in Dialectic
Definition of mudda'i and exclusion of naqid and mu'arid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mudda'i in the dialectic tradition and distinguish him from al-naqid and al-mu'arid based on the perspective of establishment.

Core Idea
The claimant is defined by intending to establish a judgment, not by mere assertion or opposition.

In this lesson, we explore the precise definition of the claimant (al-mudda'i) as given by the author. The definition emphasizes that the claimant's role is to establish a judgment, not merely to assert or oppose. This carefully excludes the refuter (naqid) and the opposer (mu'arid) because they operate from a different perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and definition
Find the term being defined (al-mudda'i) and the exact definition: 'he sets himself to establish the judgment from the perspective that it is an establishment'.
Note the key phrase: 'from the perspective that it is an establishment'.
2
Understand the exclusion clause
See how this definition excludes the naqid and mu'arid. The passage says they are not claimants because they do not aim to establish a judgment from the perspective of establishment.
They aim at negating or opposing, not establishing.
3
Compare with alternative definitions
The author says his definition is superior to the saying of 'ma' (just 'what'). Reflect on why the extra condition is needed.
4
Test the definition
Consider if every true claimant fits: they must intend to establish a judgment. Does a claimant who also refute still qualify? The definition focuses on intention.
Intention is key.

Source Text

والمدعى من هذا أولى من قول البعض (ما)، لأن المناظرة إنما تكون بين ذوات العقول ( نصب نفسه لإثبات الحكم ) أي تصدى لأن يثبت الحكم الخبري الذي تكلم به من حيث إنه إثبات ، فلا يرد ما قيل : إنه يصدق هذا التعريف على الناقض بالنقض الإجمالي والمعارض (۱) ، وهما ليسا بمدعيين في عرفهم ، لأنهما لم يتصدّيا لإثبات الحكم من حيث إنه إثبات ، بل من حيث إنه نفي لإثبات حكم تصدى لإثباته الخصم، ومن حيث إنه معارضة لدليله . ( بالدليل ، فيما إذا كان الحكم نظريا ، ( أو التنبيه ) فيما إذا كان بديهيا غير أولى . قال المصنف فيما نقل عنه : فيه مسامحة ، لأن التنبيه لا يفيد الإثبات ، كما سيجيء، تم ، تم كلامه . فإن قلت : لما كان التنبيه غير مفيد للإثبات لا يصح تعلق قوله بالتنبيه بقوله لإثبات الحكم، فكيف حكم بالمسامحة التي هي إرادة خلاف الظاهر . قلت : يمكن تصحيح التعلق بإرادة عموم المجاز في الإثبات : بأن يراد بالإثبات تمكين الحكم في ذهن المخاطب ، وذلك قد يكون بالإثبات ، وقد يكون بالإظهار (٣) . ثم عرف مولانا عصام الملة والدين (۳) في شرحه للرسالة العضدية المدَّعِي بقوله : هو من يفيد مطابقة النسبة للواقع ، وقيل : فيه نظر (1) إذ هو يصدق
And the claimant [according to this] is more appropriate than the saying of some [that it is 'what'], because disputation is only between rational beings: 'he sets himself to establish the judgment' meaning he undertakes to establish the informative judgment he spoke of from the perspective that it is an establishment. So the objection that this definition applies to the one who refutes by general refutation and the one who opposes does not stand, because they are not claimants in their terminology, since they do not undertake to establish the judgment from the perspective of establishment, but rather from the perspective of negating the establishment of a judgment that the opponent undertook to establish, and from the perspective of opposing his evidence. 'By evidence' if the judgment is theoretical, 'or by indication' if it is self-evident and not primary. The author said in what is transmitted from him: there is a leniency (musamaha) in that, because indication does not yield establishment, as will come. End of his speech. If you say: since indication does not yield establishment, it is not valid to attach his word 'by indication' to his word 'for establishment of the judgment', so how did he rule it as leniency which is intending contrary to the apparent? I say: it is possible to correct the attachment by intending a general metaphor in 'establishment': that by 'establishment' is meant enabling the judgment in the mind of the addressee, and that may be by establishment or by showing. Then our master 'Isam al-Milla wa al-Din in his commentary on al-Risala al-Abdiya defined the claimant as: he who benefits the correspondence of the attribution to reality. And it is said: there is a consideration in it because it applies to...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition of 'al-mudda'i' (the claimant). The author begins by asserting that his definition is superior to the one which simply says 'the one who sets himself to establish the judgment', because it specifies that he does so from the perspective of establishment, not from the perspective of negation or opposition. This prevents the definition from including the naqid (one who refutes the opponent's proof) and the mu'arid (one who presents a counter-argument), who are not considered claimants in this discipline. The author then explains that the claimant uses a proof (dalil) for theoretical judgments and a direct indication (tanbih) for self-evident matters. However, the author (the original text) notes that using 'tanbih' is a leniency because it does not actually prove the judgment; it merely points it out. An objection is raised: if tanbih does not establish, how can it be attached to the phrase 'establishment of the judgment'? The answer is that 'establishment' here is metaphorically used to mean 'making the judgment present in the mind', which can be done either by proof or by indicating. Finally, the commentator presents another definition by 'Isam al-Din: the claimant is one who establishes the correspondence of the attribution to reality. This definition is criticized for being overinclusive, but the passage cuts off before completing the critique.

الناقض
المعارض

A) They are not rational beings.
B) They do not intend to establish the judgment but to negate or oppose.
C) They use different methods.
D) They are not the original claimant.

Key Takeaway
The claimant is defined by the intention to establish a judgment, not by mere opposition or refutation.

The Leniency of Using Tanbih for Establishment
Explanation of tanbih and the leniency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why using tanbih as a means of establishment is considered a leniency (musamaha) and how the author resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Tanbih does not prove but indicates; calling it establishment is a metaphorical extension.

The author states that for self-evident judgments, the claimant uses tanbih (direct indication). However, he notes this is a leniency because tanbih does not actually establish the judgment. An objection is raised: how can tanbih be said to be for establishment? The author resolves this by interpreting 'establishment' metaphorically as making the judgment present in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two methods
The passage says: for theoretical judgments, the claimant uses a proof (dalil); for self-evident ones, he uses tanbih (indication). Note that tanbih is not a proof.
Self-evident matters require no logical demonstration.
2
Grasp the problem
The author is reported to have said there is a leniency (musamaha) here because tanbih does not yield establishment. If tanbih doesn't prove, how can it be linked to 'establishment'?
This is a logical tension.
3
Learn the resolution
The response: 'establishment' is used metaphorically to mean 'making the judgment present in the mind'. This can be done by proof or by direct showing.
This saves the definition.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Consider whether this metaphorical broadening is acceptable. It shows scholarly flexibility but also precision in acknowledging the leniency.

The Roles of Claimant and Questioner in Logical Debate
The commentator defines the claimant and questioner in logical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-mudda'i and al-sa'il in a dialectical exchange, and identify their subtypes (naqid, mani', mu'arid) based on the commentator's definitions.

Core Idea
Whoever sets out to prove a claim is a claimant; whoever sets out to refute it is a questioner—but the questioner may object, demand proof, or provide counter-proof.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the roles of the claimant (al-mudda'i) and the questioner (al-sa'il) are precisely defined. The commentator explains these definitions and addresses an objection about what counts as a claim. This lesson breaks down the definitions and the subtypes of the questioner, preparing you to analyze real debates in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic definition of al-mudda'i
The commentator defines al-mudda'i as one who applies himself to establish the correspondence of a predicative ratio to reality. This means the claimant affirms something about the world.
2
Identify the basic definition of al-sa'il
Al-sa'il is defined as one who sets himself to refute the claimant's judgment. The narrower meaning restricts him to the naqid (one who objects without providing proof).
The broader meaning includes the mani' (demander of proof) and the mu'arid (provider of counter-proof).
3
Distinguish the subtypes of al-sa'il
The commentator explains that al-sa'il can be general: everyone who speaks about the claimant's claim, whether as a preventer (mani'), objector (naqid), or opponent (mu'arid).
This broader definition helps in categorizing different types of interlocutors in a debate.
4
Understand the objection about parts of conditionals
An objection is raised that the definition of a claim applies to every predicative sentence, but parts of conditionals (e.g., 'if it rains') do not benefit independently. The commentator responds that the intended benefit is specifically correspondence to reality, not any benefit.
This shows the precision required in logical definitions.
5
Note the distinction between mustadill and mu'allal
The claimant, depending on the type of proof, is called mustadill (using a priori proof from cause to effect) or mu'allal (using a posteriori proof from effect to cause). The terms can be used interchangeably to mean 'one who adheres to proof'.
This distinction is important for understanding the structure of arguments in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالسَّائِلُ : مَنْ نَصَبَ نَفْسَهُ لِنَفْيه ، وقَدْ يُطلَقُ عَلَى مَا هُو أَعَمُ . على كل من قال بجمل لإفادتها كلها الصدق بالاتفاق ، ولكن بعضها لا يدعى بها الصدق، كأطراف الشرطيات، فلا يكون التعريف مطردا . أقول : : معنى كلامه أن المدَّعِي من تصدى نفسه لإفادة مطابقة النسبة الخبرية للواقع ، على أطراف الشرطيات - حين كونها أطرافا لها - ليست بجمل، ثم المدعي إن شرع في الدليل الإني يسمى مستدلا (۱) ، وإن شرع في الدليل اللمي معللا (٢) . وقد يستعمل كل منهما مقام الآخر، بمعنى المتمسك بالدليل يسمى مطلقا (۳). والسائل من نصب نفسه لنفيه ، أي لنفي الحكم الذي ادعاه المدعي و بلا نصب دليل عليه هذا يصدق على المناقض فقط ( وقد يطلق على ما هو أعم (٤) وهو كل من تكلم على ما تكلم به المدعي أعم أن يكون مانعا ، أو ناقضا ، أو معارضا . فلكونه يصدق على كل جملة خبرية وإن لم يتصد من يذكرها لإثباتها ، وأما كونه غير جامع فلأنه لا يصدق على أطراف الشرطيات لأنها لا تفيد شيئا بطريق الاستقلال فأجاب الشارح عن هذا الاعتراض بأنه ليس المراد مطلق الإفادة وإنما إفادة مطابقة النسبة الخيرية للواقع، وأطراف الشرطيات ليست بجمل حال كونها أطرافاً (1) الدليل الإني هو الاستدلال بالعلة على المعلول، كأن يستدل بالنار على وجود الحرارة . (۲) والدليل اللمي هو الاستدلال بالمعلول على العلة كالاستدلال بالدخان على وجود النار . (۳) أي قد يستعمل الدليل الاني مكان اللمي ويستعمل اللمي مكان الاني على أن المراد بكل منهما التمسك بالدليل . (٤) يعني أن السائل قد يراد منه الناقض فقط وهو الذي يبطل دليل المدعي من غير دليل وقد يراد منه معنى أعم من هذا فيشمل المانع وهو طالب الدليل ويشمل الناقض كما تقدم، ويشمل المعارض وهو الذي يقيم دليلا على خلاف ما يقيم الدليل عليه الخصم وهذا المعنى هو ما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : وهو أي السائل كل من تكلم على ما تكلم به المدعى .
The Questioner: one who sets himself to refute it, and it may be used for what is more general. For every one who speaks with sentences to all of them for their benefit truth by agreement, but some of them are not claimed to be true, like the parts of conditionals, so the definition is not comprehensive. I say: The meaning of his statement is that the Claimant is one who applies himself to benefit the correspondence of the predicative ratio to reality. The parts of conditionals – when they are parts of them – are not sentences. Then the claimant, if he proceeds with the a priori proof (dalil inni), is called a mustadill (1), and if he proceeds with the a posteriori proof (dalil limmi), is called a mu'allal (2). And each may be used in place of the other, meaning the one who adheres to proof is called mutlaq (3). The Questioner is one who sets himself to refute it, i.e., to negate the judgment that the claimant claimed, without presenting a proof for it. This applies only to the naqid (objector). And it may be used for what is more general (4), i.e., everyone who speaks about what the claimant spoke about, whether he is a mani' (preventer), or naqid, or mu'arid (opponent). For it applies to every predicative sentence even if its mentioner does not intend to prove it. And as for it not being comprehensive, it is because it does not apply to the parts of conditionals since they do not benefit anything independently. So the commentator answered this objection by saying that the intended meaning is not absolute benefit, but rather benefit of correspondence of the predicative ratio to reality. And the parts of conditionals, when they are parts, are not sentences. (1) The a priori proof is reasoning from cause to effect, such as reasoning from fire to the existence of heat. (2) The a posteriori proof is reasoning from effect to cause, such as reasoning from smoke to the existence of fire. (3) That is, the a priori proof may be used in place of the a posteriori and vice versa, meaning by each the adherence to proof. (4) He means that al-sa'il may intend only the naqid, who invalidates the claimant's proof without [his own] proof, and it may intend a more general meaning than this, so it includes the mani' who is the demander of proof, and it includes the naqid as previously mentioned, and it includes the mu'arid who provides a proof contrary to what the opponent provides a proof for. And this meaning is what the commentator indicated by his statement: i.e., al-sa'il is everyone who speaks about what the claimant spoke about.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining two key roles in logical debate: al-mudda'i (the claimant) and al-sa'il (the questioner). The commentator begins by defining al-sa'il as 'one who sets himself to refute it' and notes that the term can be used more broadly. He then addresses an objection about the definition of a sentence (jumla) being limited to those that can be claimed true, arguing that the parts of conditionals (atraf al-shartiyyat) are not independent sentences. The claimant (al-mudda'i) is defined as one who seeks to establish the correspondence of the predicative ratio to reality. Depending on his method, he is called mustadill if he uses an a priori proof (dalil inni) or mu'allal if he uses an a posteriori proof (dalil limmi), though each term can substitute for the other. The questioner (al-sa'il) is specifically one who refutes without providing evidence—this is the naqid (objector). But the term can be more general, including the mani' (preventer, who demands proof) and the mu'arid (opponent, who provides a counter-proof). The commentator clarifies that the definition of al-sa'il is not comprehensive because it does not cover the parts of conditionals, but the response is that the intended 'benefit' is not absolute but specific to correspondence to reality. The footnotes explain the types of proof and the general meaning of al-sa'il.

الدليل اللمي

In a logical debate, what distinguishes al-mudda'i from al-sa'il according to the commentator?
A) Al-mudda'i is the one who refutes, al-sa'il is the one who proves.
B) Al-mudda'i seeks to establish a claim, al-sa'il seeks to refute it.
C) Al-mudda'i always uses a priori proof, al-sa'il always uses a posteriori proof.
D) Al-mudda'i is always a mu'allal, al-sa'il is always a naqid.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectic, the claimant (al-mudda'i) bears the burden of proof, while the questioner (al-sa'il) may object, demand proof, or provide a counter-argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about divine attributes, Zaid argues: 'God has eternal knowledge.' Amr responds: 'Prove that knowledge is not an accident.' Identify: Who is al-mudda'i? Who is al-sa'il? Is Amr a naqid, mani', or mu'arid? Explain using the commentator's definitions.
?

The Claim (al-Da'wa) and Its Names in Dialectic
The commentator defines al-da'wa and lists its synonyms based on logical stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four names of al-da'wa depending on the stage of reasoning and distinguish it from al-matlab.

Core Idea
A claim is called question, result, or rule depending on where it stands in the argument process.

In dialectical reasoning, the same proposition can be referred to by different names depending on its role in the investigation. The commentator explains that al-da'wa (the claim) is a proposition that includes the judgment intended to be proven. When it is under investigation, it is called a mas'ala (question); when derived from evidence, a natija (result); and when it becomes a universal principle, a qa'ida (rule). He also contrasts da'wa with the broader term al-matlab, which includes both conceptual and assentive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of al-da'wa
Read the opening statement: 'al-da'wa is what includes the judgment intended to be proven.' Note that it is a proposition (qadiyya) and the judgment is the central part.
The judgment is what is being claimed, such as 'the world is created' or 'every intoxicant is forbidden'.
2
Recognize the different names based on perspective
The commentator lists: mas'ala, mabhath, natija, qa'ida, qanun. Each corresponds to a stage: under investigation (question), derived from evidence (result), or applied universally (rule). Map each term to its stage.
The term mabhath is synonymous with mas'ala, and qanun is synonymous with qa'ida.
3
Distinguish between da'wa and matlab
Al-matlab includes both conceptual (tasawwuri) and assentive (tasdiqi) knowledge, while da'wa is only assentive. For example, 'the essence of man' is a conceptual matlab, not a da'wa.
Da'wa always involves an assertion (tasdiq), whereas matlab can be a concept like 'human'.
4
Understand the condition for debate
The commentator notes that if the claimed judgment is self-evident (badihi awwali), it cannot be a da'wa in a debate because debate only occurs when proof or indication is needed. Disputing self-evident truths is obstinacy, not legitimate debate.
This refines the definition by excluding obvious truths from the scope of dialectical claims.

And al-Da'wa (the claim) is: what includes the judgment intended to be proven, and that is called a question (mas'ala), a research topic (mabhath), a result (natija), a rule (qa'ida), and a law (qanun). And al-Matlab (the sought) is more general, either conceptual (tasawwuri) or assentive (tasdiqi), and it is also called matlab. And it may be said that al-matlab is what is sought for concepts. And al-da'wa is what (i.e., a proposition) includes the judgment, as a whole includes a part (1), intended to be proven by evidence or shown by indication. And in it, it may be that the claimed judgment is self-evident and primary (2), and it can be said: if the judgment is like that, the debate (munazara) does not take place, because only a disputant or an obstinate person denies it. And it is called, from the perspective that the question or investigation is directed at it or at its evidence, a question and a research topic; and from the perspective that it is derived from the evidence, a result; and from the perspective that it may be universal, a rule (3) and a law. And al-matlab is more general (4) than al-da'wa, whether conceptual, like the essence of man. (4) (1) The commentator indicates that the proposition is a whole that includes parts, among them the judgment, which are the concept of the subject (mahkum 'alayhi), the concept of the predicate (mahkum bihi), and the concept of the relation. This is based on the view that assent (tasdiq) is composed of concepts and a judgment, whether that judgment is a concept as some logicians hold or an act as others hold. (2) The commentator indicates by this that al-da'wa is more general than what he mentioned, because it does not include the claimed if it is self-evident and primary, but he answered that the intended meaning of da'wa here is that which can be subject to debate, and it is only in a matter intended to be proven by evidence if it is not established, or to be indicated if it is hidden. As for disputing about the self-evident primary, it is not debate but only obstinacy or disputation. (3) Al-da'wa, which includes the judgment intended to be proven, has different names depending on the purpose. If it is a subject of investigation, it is called a question and a research topic; if we engage in inference upon it and then benefit from the evidence, it is called a result; and when the inference is complete and it becomes a universal matter from which rulings on its particulars are derived, it is called a rule or a law. For example, research about the rule of the subject (fa'il): in this state it is called a question. If we derive its rule from inference and extract it, it is called a result. If we apply this result that was derived from inference to every subject, e.g., we say: Muhammad—the one who stood—Muhammad is a subject and every subject is nominative, so Muhammad is nominative, it is called a rule. (4) Because al-da'wa applies only to assentive information, whereas al-matlab applies to information =
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines al-da'wa (the claim) and distinguishes it from al-matlab (the sought). The commentator begins by stating that al-da'wa is a proposition (qadiyya) that includes the judgment intended to be proven. Depending on the aspect under consideration, the same claim can be called a mas'ala (question), mabhath (research topic), natija (result), qa'ida (rule), or qanun (law). He then notes that al-matlab is more general, covering both conceptual (tasawwuri) and assentive (tasdiqi) knowledge, while al-da'wa is only for assentive. The commentator addresses an apparent limitation: if the claimed judgment is self-evident (badihi awwali), debate does not occur; thus da'wa here refers only to matters that can be debated, i.e., those requiring proof or indication. The footnotes elaborate: the proposition is a whole with parts (subject, predicate, relation); the different names depend on the stage of investigation (as a subject of inquiry, as a derived conclusion, or as a universal rule). An example from Arabic grammar is given: research on the rule of the subject (fa'il) is a question, deriving its rule gives a result, and applying it to all subjects (like Muhammad) makes it a rule.

الدعوى
المطلوب
مسألة
نتيجة
قاعدة

Key Takeaway
A claim in dialectic is not static; its name changes from question to result to rule depending on where it is in the reasoning process.

What is al-Man'? Defining the Objection in Jadal
Definition of al-man' and its classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-man' and distinguish it from summary naqd and common misinterpretations.

Core Idea
The objection is not a dismissal — it is a precise request for evidence on a single premise.

This lesson unpacks the technical definition of al-man' (the objection) in the Islamic dialectical tradition. You will learn exactly what qualifies as a valid man', why it is also called detailed naqd, and how the author clarifies the definition to avoid circularity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum and its definition
Find the Arabic term 'المنع' and the phrase 'طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة'. Identify the two key components: a request (طلب) and the object of request (evidence for a specific premise).
The definition is operational: it tells you what the objector does.
2
Examine the qualification of 'specific'
The author adds 'معينة' (specific) to the premise. Without it, the definition would be too broad. What type of objection would that include that is not intended? Consider the difference between objecting to a single premise versus rejecting the whole argument.
This is to exclude summary naqd (النقض الإجمالي).
3
Analyze the correction about the pronoun
The author explains why he did not phrase the definition as 'طلب الدليل على مقدمة دليله'. Work out the erroneous implication: it would suggest demanding evidence for a premise of the very evidence being requested, leading to a circular or infinite regress.
The phrasing avoids misunderstanding.
4
Distinguish from summary naqd
Compare the definition of man' with the concept of 'naqd ijmali' (summary rejection). How does specifying 'specific premise' and the synonym 'naqd tafsili' clearly separate the two?
Man' is a detailed, premise-level challenge.
5
Preview the application on both premises
The passage notes that man' can target both premises of an argument in detail. Imagine a debater objecting to both the major and minor premises. How would that unfold?
The example is cut off, but the logic is clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المَنْعُ : طَلبُ الدَّليلِ عَلَى مُقدِّمةٍ مُعَيَّةٍ ، ويُسَمَّى مُناقضَةً، وَنَقْضًا تَفْصِيليا . ترك إضافة المقدمة إلى ضمير الدليل لأنه يوهم ظاهره أن المطلوب طلب دليل على مقدمة ذلك الدليل المطلوب ، وليس الأمر كذلك وقيدها بالمعينة لئلا يرد النقض بالنقض الإجمالي . قيل : المنع قد يرد على كلتا مقدمتي الدليل على التفصيل، كما إذا قال ...
Al-Man' (objection/denial): requesting evidence for a specific premise, and it is called munada and detailed naqd. He omitted adding the premise to the pronoun of the evidence because its apparent meaning would suggest that the request is for evidence for a premise of that requested evidence, and that is not the case. He qualified it with 'specific' to avoid the objection being confused with the summary naqd. It is said: the objection may be directed at both premises of the evidence in detail, as when one says ...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines al-man' (the objection or denial) in logical debate. It is the act of requesting evidence (dalil) for a specific premise (muqaddima mu'ayyana). The term is also called munada and detailed naqd (naqd tafsili). The author explains why the definition does not add the premise as an annex to the pronoun of 'evidence' (i.e., not 'requesting evidence for its premise'): because that might wrongly imply that the requested evidence itself is for a premise of that same evidence, which is circular. He also specifies 'specific premise' to distinguish this from summary naqd (naqd ijmali), which rejects the entire argument. The passage then notes that man' can occur on both premises of an argument in detail, as in the example (which is cut off).

C) Because the premise is not specific.

Key Takeaway
A valid objection (man') is a precise demand for evidence on one specific premise, not a blanket rejection of the entire argument.

The Direction of Implication: Antecedent and Consequent
Defines الملزوم واللازم with commentary on implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the antecedent (ملزوم) and consequent (لازم) in any given implication, including reciprocal cases.

Core Idea
Implication has direction: the antecedent necessitates the consequent, but sometimes the relation is two-way.

In dialectical argumentation, we often deal with implications — statements like 'if A then B'. This lesson defines the two sides of any implication: what necessitates (ملزوم) and what is necessitated (لازم). The commentary illustrates with examples like fire and heat, and notes that implication can be reciprocal, as in fever and heat. Understanding this directional relationship is the first step to analyzing objections in debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in the implication
In any implication, there are two terms: one that necessitates and one that is necessitated. For example, in 'fire necessitates heat', fire is the antecedent and heat is the consequent.
The passage uses the example of fire and heat.
2
Check if the implication is one-way or two-way
If the relation can be reversed, it is reciprocal (استلزام من الجانبين). The passage gives the example of fever and heat: heat can be considered antecedent and fever consequent, or vice versa.
Reciprocal implication is common in natural pairs.
3
Apply the distinction to any given statement
Given a statement like 'the obligation of Hajj implies ability', identify which is the antecedent and which is the consequent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأوَّلُ يُسَمَّى مَلْزُومًا والثاني يُسمى لازما (۱) يعني أن الملزوم هو ما يقتضي غيره كاقتضاء النار للحرارة ، واللازم هو ما يكون مقتضى غيره كالحرارة للنار . وقد يكون الاستلزام من الجانبين (۲) كمرض الحمى والحرارة. فإننا يتأتى لنا أن نتصور أن الحرارة ملزوم والحمى لازم كما يتأتى لنا أن نتصور العكس، وكما في الأشياء وماهياتها فيصح أن تكون الأشياء علة في ماهياتها ويصح العكس. فأي يتصور مقتضيا يسمى ملزوما ، وأي يتصور مقتضى يسمى لازما . ثم اعلم أنه قدس سره بين الملزوم واللازم، ولم يبين المدلول مع الدليل، لأنه كثيرا ما يرد المنع على بطلان اللازم، كما يرد على أصل الملازمة ، ولهذا أردف تعريفها بتعريف المنع وقال : ( المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة ويسمى ) ذلك الطلب و مناقضة ونقضا تفصيليا أيضا (۳) النقض قسمان : نقض إجمالي ؛ ونقض تفصيلي، فالنقض الإجمالي هو إبطال الدليل بعد تمامه بالتخلف ؛ أي تخلف المدلول عن دليله . أو باستلزامه المحال والنقض التفصيلي يساوي المنع . وهو طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة كما ذكره المصنف هنا . كما يسمى منعا . ترك إضافة المقدمة إلى ضمير الدليل لأنه يوهم ظاهره أن المطلوب طلب دليل على مقدمة ذلك الدليل المطلوب ، وليس الأمر كذلك وقيدها بالمعينة لئلا يرد النقض بالنقض الإجمالي .
The first is called the antecedent (ملزوم) and the second is called the consequent (لازم) (1) meaning that the antecedent is that which necessitates something else, like the necessitation of fire for heat, and the consequent is that which is necessitated by something else, like heat for fire. And implication (استلزام) can be from both sides (2) like fever and heat. For we can conceive of heat as antecedent and fever as consequent, and we can conceive of the opposite, and like things and their quidities, it is valid for things to be causes in their quidities and the opposite is also valid. So whichever is conceived as necessitating is called antecedent, and whichever is conceived as necessitated is called consequent. Then know that he (may Allah sanctify his soul) explained the antecedent and the consequent, but did not explain the indicated (مدلول) with the proof, because the objection (منع) often targets the falsehood of the consequent, just as it targets the principle of implication, and therefore he appended its definition to the definition of objection and said: (Objection is the request for proof for a specific premise) and that request is also called contradiction (مناقضة) and detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي) (3) Refutation is of two types: general refutation (نقض إجمالي) and detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي). General refutation is the invalidation of the proof after its completion by non-fulfillment (تخلف), i.e., the indicated failing to follow from its proof, or by it implying an impossibility. Detailed refutation equals objection, and it is the request for proof for a specific premise as mentioned by the author here. as it is also called objection (منع). He omitted adding the premise to the pronoun of the proof because its apparent meaning would suggest that the requested is the request for proof for a premise of that requested proof, and this is not the case. And he qualified it with 'specific' to prevent confusion with the general refutation (نقض إجمالي).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms ملزوم (antecedent) and لازم (consequent) in the context of implication (استلزام), then links them to the definition of منع (objection) as used in jadal (dialectical debate). The commentator explains that the antecedent is what necessitates something else (e.g., fire necessitates heat), while the consequent is what is necessitated (e.g., heat is necessitated by fire). He notes that implication can be reciprocal (e.g., fever and heat can be seen either way). The passage then discusses why the author of the matn (قدس سره) defined منع after defining ملزوم and لازم: because an objection often targets either the falsehood of the consequent or the principle of implication itself. The definition of منع is given: 'request for proof for a specific premise.' This is also called detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي), as opposed to general refutation (نقض إجمالي), which invalidates a proof after completion by showing non-fulfillment or impossibility. The commentator clarifies that the premise is not attached to the pronoun of the proof to avoid the misconception that one is requesting proof for a premise of that same proof. The qualification 'specific' prevents confusion with general refutation. This lesson establishes the foundational terminology for understanding objections in dialectical argumentation.

لازم

Objection (منع) and Types of Refutation in Jadal
Defines المنع and distinguishes types of refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the concept of objection (منع) in a dialectical argument, and distinguish it from general refutation (نقض إجمالي).

Core Idea
Objection is not denial; it is a request for proof for a specific premise.

In jadal (dialectical debate), when a claimant presents a proof, the opponent can respond by objecting to a specific premise. This objection (منع) is defined as 'the request for proof for a specific premise'. The passage distinguishes this from general refutation (نقض إجمالي), which invalidates the entire proof after completion by showing non-fulfillment or impossibility. Mastering this tool is essential for conducting rigorous theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise under question
In a dialectical argument, the objector targets a specific premise within the proof, not the whole argument. The passage defines منع as 'طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة'.
The premise must be specific to differentiate from general refutation.
2
Distinguish between detailed and general refutation
Detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي) equals objection — it requests proof for a premise. General refutation (نقض إجمالي) invalidates the entire proof after it is complete, either by showing that the consequent does not follow (تخلف) or that the proof leads to an impossibility.
The passage lists the two types of refutation.
3
Practice by formulating an objection
Given a proof, e.g., 'The world is originated, and everything originated has a cause, so the world has a cause.' An objection could be: 'Request proof for the premise that everything originated has a cause.'
The objection does not deny the premise; it only asks for evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأوَّلُ يُسَمَّى مَلْزُومًا والثاني يُسمى لازما (۱) يعني أن الملزوم هو ما يقتضي غيره كاقتضاء النار للحرارة ، واللازم هو ما يكون مقتضى غيره كالحرارة للنار . وقد يكون الاستلزام من الجانبين (۲) كمرض الحمى والحرارة. فإننا يتأتى لنا أن نتصور أن الحرارة ملزوم والحمى لازم كما يتأتى لنا أن نتصور العكس، وكما في الأشياء وماهياتها فيصح أن تكون الأشياء علة في ماهياتها ويصح العكس. فأي يتصور مقتضيا يسمى ملزوما ، وأي يتصور مقتضى يسمى لازما . ثم اعلم أنه قدس سره بين الملزوم واللازم، ولم يبين المدلول مع الدليل، لأنه كثيرا ما يرد المنع على بطلان اللازم، كما يرد على أصل الملازمة ، ولهذا أردف تعريفها بتعريف المنع وقال : ( المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة ويسمى ) ذلك الطلب و مناقضة ونقضا تفصيليا أيضا (۳) النقض قسمان : نقض إجمالي ؛ ونقض تفصيلي، فالنقض الإجمالي هو إبطال الدليل بعد تمامه بالتخلف ؛ أي تخلف المدلول عن دليله . أو باستلزامه المحال والنقض التفصيلي يساوي المنع . وهو طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة كما ذكره المصنف هنا . كما يسمى منعا . ترك إضافة المقدمة إلى ضمير الدليل لأنه يوهم ظاهره أن المطلوب طلب دليل على مقدمة ذلك الدليل المطلوب ، وليس الأمر كذلك وقيدها بالمعينة لئلا يرد النقض بالنقض الإجمالي .
The first is called the antecedent (ملزوم) and the second is called the consequent (لازم) (1) meaning that the antecedent is that which necessitates something else, like the necessitation of fire for heat, and the consequent is that which is necessitated by something else, like heat for fire. And implication (استلزام) can be from both sides (2) like fever and heat. For we can conceive of heat as antecedent and fever as consequent, and we can conceive of the opposite, and like things and their quidities, it is valid for things to be causes in their quidities and the opposite is also valid. So whichever is conceived as necessitating is called antecedent, and whichever is conceived as necessitated is called consequent. Then know that he (may Allah sanctify his soul) explained the antecedent and the consequent, but did not explain the indicated (مدلول) with the proof, because the objection (منع) often targets the falsehood of the consequent, just as it targets the principle of implication, and therefore he appended its definition to the definition of objection and said: (Objection is the request for proof for a specific premise) and that request is also called contradiction (مناقضة) and detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي) (3) Refutation is of two types: general refutation (نقض إجمالي) and detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي). General refutation is the invalidation of the proof after its completion by non-fulfillment (تخلف), i.e., the indicated failing to follow from its proof, or by it implying an impossibility. Detailed refutation equals objection, and it is the request for proof for a specific premise as mentioned by the author here. as it is also called objection (منع). He omitted adding the premise to the pronoun of the proof because its apparent meaning would suggest that the requested is the request for proof for a premise of that requested proof, and this is not the case. And he qualified it with 'specific' to prevent confusion with the general refutation (نقض إجمالي).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms ملزوم (antecedent) and لازم (consequent) in the context of implication (استلزام), then links them to the definition of منع (objection) as used in jadal (dialectical debate). The commentator explains that the antecedent is what necessitates something else (e.g., fire necessitates heat), while the consequent is what is necessitated (e.g., heat is necessitated by fire). He notes that implication can be reciprocal (e.g., fever and heat can be seen either way). The passage then discusses why the author of the matn (قدس سره) defined منع after defining ملزوم and لازم: because an objection often targets either the falsehood of the consequent or the principle of implication itself. The definition of منع is given: 'request for proof for a specific premise.' This is also called detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي), as opposed to general refutation (نقض إجمالي), which invalidates a proof after completion by showing non-fulfillment or impossibility. The commentator clarifies that the premise is not attached to the pronoun of the proof to avoid the misconception that one is requesting proof for a premise of that same proof. The qualification 'specific' prevents confusion with general refutation. This lesson establishes the foundational terminology for understanding objections in dialectical argumentation.

Key Terms
منع
نقض إجمالي
نقض تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between detailed refutation (نقض تفصيلي) and general refutation (نقض إجمالي)?
A) Detailed refutation invalidates the entire proof, while general refutation targets a premise.
B) Detailed refutation is synonymous with objection (منع) and requests proof for a premise, while general refutation invalidates the proof after completion.
C) General refutation is also called objection (منع), while detailed refutation is something else.
D) There is no difference; they are two terms for the same thing.

Key Takeaway
Objection (منع) is a request for proof for a specific premise, distinct from general refutation which invalidates the entire proof.
