البَحْث الأول
في طَريقِ البَحْثِ وتَرتِيبُهُ الطَّبِيعِي
رحمه الله
ظاهرة لا تكون مجهولة إلا لمن كان أسوأ الحال ثم قال المصنف - قدس الله سره - في الحاشية : ثم اعلم أن المعلل مادام في تعريف الأقوال والتحرير لا يتوجه عليه المنع ، كما إذا قال المعلل : الزكاة واجبة في حلي النساء عند أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - وليست بواجبة عند الشافعي - رحمه فلا يقال له : لم قلت أنها واجبة ؟ لأنه ذكر القول بطريق الحكاية لا بطريق الادعاء، ولا دخل في الحكايات إلا إذا نقل شيئا واخطأ في النقل، فحينئذ يجوز طلب تصحيح النقل ، أو عرف شيئا ولم يكن تعريفه جامعا أو مانعا ، فيجوز أن يطلب الطرد والعكس، فلا يجوز الدخل إذا كان جامعا ومانعا تم كلامه والمراد بكونه جامعا ومانعا علم المخاطب بهما، لأنه كثيرا ما يكون الحد جامعا ولا يعلم السائل فيطلب، ويجوز طلبه بالاتفاق
فلنشرع ) أي لما فرغنا من بيان المقدمة، وبيان أجزاء البحث ، فلنشرع بعد ذكر التعريفات وبيان أجزاء البحث في الأبحاث ) وهي تسعة :
البحث الأول
في ) بيان ( طريق البحث وترتيبه الطبيعي ) في التقديم والتأخير . والترتيب في اللغة : جعل كل شيء في مرتبته ، وفي الاصطلاح : جعل الأشياء المتعددة بحيث يطلق عليها الاسم الواحد ويكون لبعضها نسبة إلى البعض بالتقديم والتأخير ، وأراد بالترتيب الطبيعي الترتيب الذي تقتضي طبيعة البحث أن يكون عليه، وهو ما فصله بقوله : يلتزم الخصم البيان بعد الاستفسار أي بعد ما يطلب بيانه من تعيين المدعى ، لأنه لو اشتغل بالبيان قبل الطلب يعد عبثا ويؤاخذ أي الخصم إذا كان على صيغة المجهول أو السائل إذا كان مبنيا للفاعل بتصحيح النقل، أي بيان صحة نسبة ما نسب إليه من كتاب أو ثقة إن نقل شيئًا، مثاله إذا قال ناقل : قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : النية ليست بشرط في الوضوء، يقول السائل : ما النية ، وما الشرط ، وما الوضوء ؟ فبعد ما بين تعاريفها كما يؤاخذ بتصحيح النقل بأن يقال له : من أين تنقل أنه قال أبو حنيفة - رحمه الله - ذلك فيقول الناقل : قد صرح به في الهداية لكن في زماننا لما نشأ الكذب والمجادلة والمكابرة لا يكفى هذا القول، بل لابد من أن يرى نقله ثم عطف على قوله بتصحيح النقل الخ بقوله وبالتنبيه أو الدليل إن ادعى فما باله لو كان في زماننا ؟
Let us now proceed to the researches: The first research on the method of research and its natural order. Rahmatullah 'alayh. An obvious matter not unknown except to one in the worst state. Then the author (may God sanctify his secret) said in the gloss: Know that as long as the person providing evidence is defining the statements and writing them out, objection does not apply to him, as when the person providing evidence says: Zakat is obligatory on women's jewellery according to Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) and not obligatory according to al-Shafi'i (may God have mercy on him). So it is not said to him: Why did you say it is obligatory? Because he mentioned the statement by way of narration, not by way of assertion. And one does not enter into narrations except when he narrates something and errs in the narration; then it is permissible to demand correction of the transmission. Or he defined something and his definition was not comprehensive or exclusive; then it is permissible to demand al-tard and al-'aks. So it is not permissible to object if it is comprehensive and exclusive. End of his words. And what is meant by it being comprehensive and exclusive is the knowledge of the addressee of them, because often a definition is comprehensive but the questioner does not know, so he asks. And asking for it is permissible by agreement. "Let us now proceed" means: after we have finished explaining the introduction and explaining the parts of the research, let us proceed, after mentioning the definitions and explaining the parts of the research, to the researches, which are nine: The first research: In explaining the method of research and its natural order in priority and posteriority. And al-tartib (order) linguistically is placing each thing in its rank; and terminologically is placing multiple things such that the same name applies to them and some have a relation to others in priority and posteriority. And by "natural order" he means the order that the nature of research requires it to be, which is what he detailed by his statement: The opponent is obligated to explain after inquiry, i.e., after the request for explanation of the specification of the claim, because if he busied himself with explanation before the request, it would be considered frivolous. And he is held responsible (or the questioner, if active) for correcting the transmission, i.e., explaining the correctness of the attribution of what is attributed to him from a book or trustworthy person, if he transmitted something. Its example: if a transmitter says: Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) said: Intention is not a condition in ablution; the questioner says: What is intention? What is condition? What is ablution? After he explains their definitions, he is also held responsible for correcting the transmission by saying to him: From where do you transmit that Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) said that? So the transmitter says: He stated it explicitly in al-Hidayah, but in our time, due to the emergence of lying, disputation, and stubbornness, this statement is not sufficient; rather, he must show his transmission. Then he appended to his statement "correction of the transmission, etc." his statement "and by notification or proof if he claims" – so what would it be like if it were in our time?
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Rashidiyya expands on the first research concerning the method of research and its natural order in debate. The commentary begins by explaining that the person providing evidence (al-mu'allil) is not to be objected to as long as he is in the process of defining the statements and writing them out. An example is given: if the mu'allil says that zakat is obligatory on women's jewellery according to Abu Hanifa but not according to al-Shafi'i, he is merely narrating, not asserting. Therefore, one cannot ask 'Why did you say it is obligatory?' because it is a narration. Objection is only allowed if he errs in the transmission or if his definition is not comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'). However, if the definition is comprehensive and exclusive, no objection is permitted. The commentary clarifies that 'comprehensive and exclusive' is understood subjectively—i.e., as far as the questioner knows—since often a definition is comprehensive but the questioner may not realize it, so he can still ask for clarification, and that is permissible by consensus. The passage then proceeds to define 'natural order' (al-tartib al-tabi'i): linguistically, it is placing each thing in its rank; terminologically, it is arranging multiple things under a single name with a relation of priority and posteriority. In the context of research, the natural order is that the opponent (khasm) is obligated to explain after inquiry (istifsar), meaning after the questioner requests specification of the claim. If the opponent explains before being asked, it is considered frivolous. Additionally, the opponent is held responsible for correcting any transmission he makes. An example from fiqh is given: if a transmitter says Abu Hanifa ruled that intention is not a condition for ablution (wudu'), the questioner may first ask for definitions of key terms (intention, condition, ablution). After the transmitter defines them, he is then asked to verify the source of his transmission, e.g., from al-Hidayah. The commentator laments that in his own time, due to prevalent lying and disputation, a mere reference is insufficient; the transmitter must show the actual text. This highlights the importance of establishing reliable transmission in dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
المعلل
السائل
تصحيح النقل
الترتيب الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The opponent explains everything at once.
B) The opponent explains only after the questioner requests specification of the claim.
C) The questioner presents the claim first.
D) Neither party speaks until the moderator gives permission.

Key Takeaway
Natural order in debate requires that the opponent explain only after the questioner has requested specification of the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are the questioner in a debate. The opponent immediately starts reciting proofs for God's existence. What is the problem according to natural order? Formulate the correct procedure: what should the opponent do first?

Hint vs Proof: The Third Duty of the Reason-Giver in Debate
Commentary explains the third duty: providing a hint for hidden obvious claims and proof for theoretical unknown claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a 'hint' (tanbīh) for hidden obvious claims and a 'proof' (dalīl) for theoretical unknown claims, and apply this distinction in a jadal scenario.

Core Idea
In debate, the type of evidence required depends on the claim's epistemic status: hidden obvious needs a hint, theoretical unknown needs a proof.

When you make a claim in a debate (jadal), the opponent may ask you for evidence. But not all claims need the same kind of evidence. If the claim is self-evident but not immediately clear, a simple hint suffices. If it is a theoretical truth that is unknown, you must provide a full syllogistic proof. This lesson unpacks this crucial distinction from the Rāshidiyya commentary on the duties of the reason-giver (al-muʿallil).

Guided Path
1
Identify the epistemic status of the claim
Determine whether the claim is obvious (badīhī) or theoretical (naẓarī). The obvious is self-evident; the theoretical requires reasoning. Note that the source adds nuances: obvious can be hidden, theoretical can be unknown.
The passage contrasts badīhī khafiyy (hidden obvious) with naẓarī majhūl (theoretical unknown).
2
Check if the obvious claim is hidden
If the claim is obvious but not immediately apparent, it is 'hidden obvious'. In that case, the opponent may legitimately request a hint (tanbīh) to make it clear. The hint need not be a full syllogism.
Example from the passage: 'a true reality of realities is established' – a hidden obvious claim.
3
Recognize when a full proof is required
If the claim is theoretical and not yet known (naẓarī majhūl), the opponent must provide a full syllogistic proof (dalīl). This proof must be a valid argument that establishes the claim.
Example from the passage: 'the world is originated' – a theoretical unknown claim requiring a categorical syllogism.
4
Apply to the examples given
In the first example, the people of truth claim realities are established; the sophist asks for a hint. In the second, the mutakallim claims the world is originated; the philosopher asks for a proof. Confirm that the response matches the requirement.
The hint for the first is based on observation or the opponent's own nature; the proof for the second is a categorical syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالتنبيه أو الدليل إن ادعى بديهيا خفيًا أو نظريًا مجهولًا، أي يؤاخذ بالتنبيه إن ادعى بديهيا خفيًا، كما إذا قال: أهل الحق: حقيقة من حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، فيقول السوفسطائي: بأي تنبيه تقول؟ فيقول: لأنا نشاهد المشاهدات فلو لم تكن ثابتة لما نشاهدها، أو لأنك حقيقة من الحقائق، فلو لم تكن ثابتا لما تطلب منا التنبيه. ويؤاخذ بالدليل إن ادعى نظريًا مجهولًا، كما إذا قال المتكلم: العالم حادث، يقول الحكيم: بأي دليل تقول ذلك؟ فيقول: لأنه متغير، وكل متغير حادث، فهو حادث. ووجه تقييد البديهي بكونه خفيًا، والنظري بكونه مجهولًا لا يخفى.
(1) من وظائف المعلل أن يفسر المفردات. وأن يصحح النقل بعد الطلب من السائل وإلا عد بيانه أو تصحيحه عيثا كما تقدم. وكذلك من وظيفته أن ينبه على البديهي الحفني أو يدلل على المجهول النظري. وقد مثل الشارح للأول بقول أهل الحق: حقيقة من حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، فيطلب منه السوفسطائي المنكر لحقائق الأشياء تنبيها على هذه الدعوى. ومثل للثاني بقول المتكلم: العالم حادث. فيطلب منه الحكيم دليلا على هذا وخص البديهي بكونه خفيا، لأنه معلوم ولكن قد يكون فيه خفاء فقط فيطلب السائل من المعلل إزالة الخفاء وخص النظري بكونه مجهولا. لأنه إن لم يكن مجهولا لا يطلب الدليل.
And with a hint or proof if he claims a hidden obvious thing or a theoretical unknown thing, i.e., he is held to a hint if he claims a hidden obvious thing, such as when he says: The people of truth: a true reality of realities is established. The sophist says: By what hint do you say? He says: Because we observe observable things, and if they were not established we would not observe them, or because you are a reality of realities, and if you were not established you would not demand a hint from us. And he is held to a proof if he claims a theoretical unknown thing, such as when the mutakallim says: The world is originated. The philosopher says: By what proof do you say that? He says: Because it is changing, and everything changing is originated, so it is originated. And the reason for restricting the obvious to being hidden, and the theoretical to being unknown, is not hidden.
(1) Among the functions of the reason-giver is to explain singular terms, and to correct the transmission after the questioner's request, otherwise his explanation or correction would be considered frivolous as explained. Likewise, among his functions is to alert to the hidden obvious or to provide proof for the theoretical unknown. The commentator gave an example of the first with the statement of the people of truth: a true reality of realities is established, so the sophist who denies realities demands from him a hint for this claim. And he gave an example of the second with the mutakallim's statement: the world is originated, so the philosopher demands from him a proof for that. And he specified the obvious as hidden because it is known but may have some obscurity, so the questioner asks the reason-giver to remove the obscurity, and he specified the theoretical as unknown because if it were not unknown, no proof would be demanded.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage explains the third duty of the opponent in debate: if he makes a claim that is obviously true but not immediately clear (بديهي خفي), he must provide a 'hint' (تنبيه) to make it apparent. If the claim is theoretical and unknown (نظري مجهول), he must provide a full proof (دليل). The commentator gives two examples. For the hidden obvious, the people of truth claim that realities are established; the sophist asks for a hint, and the response is that observing observable things proves it. For the theoretical unknown, the mutakallim claims the world is originated; the philosopher asks for a proof, and the mutakallim provides a syllogism: the world is changing, everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated. The commentator then explains why the obvious is qualified as 'hidden': because it is known but may have some subtlety, so the questioner seeks clarification. The theoretical is qualified as 'unknown' because if it were known, no proof would be demanded. The footnote reiterates the functions of the reason-giver (المعلل) and emphasizes that the request for hint or proof is legitimate according to the nature of the claim.

Key Terms
التنبيه (al-tanbīh)
الدليل (al-dalīl)
بديهي خفي (badīhī khafiyy)
نظري مجهول (naẓarī majhūl)

Key Takeaway
The epistemic status of a claim determines the type of evidence required in debate: hidden obvious claims need a hint, theoretical unknown claims need a proof.

Objecting to a Premise and Invalidating Its Support
Commentary explains the procedure when the opponent objects to a specific premise with or without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical procedure for responding to an objection to a premise by distinguishing objections with and without evidence, and by explaining the steps of invalidating the support after establishing equivalence.

Core Idea
An objection with evidence must be met by invalidating that evidence, but only after proving it is equivalent to the premise's negation.

In jadal (dialectical debate), after a proof is presented, the opponent may object to a specific premise. The objection can be supported by evidence (sanad) or not. This lesson teaches the proper response: invalidating the evidence (ibṭāl al-sanad) after establishing equivalence (ithbāt al-tasāwī) between the evidence and the negation of the objected premise. Without this procedure, objections would be arbitrary and debates would lack rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objections
The passage states that an objection to a specific premise can be 'with evidence' (مع السند) or 'without evidence' (مجردًا عنه). Recognize that the response differs: with evidence, you invalidate the evidence; without, you directly defend the premise.
2
Locate the example in the passage
The mutakallim argues that everything changing is originated. The philosopher objects: 'We do not accept that everything changing is originated' and gives evidence: 'it is not impossible that some changing thing is eternal.' This is an objection with evidence.
The philosopher's evidence is the possibility claim.
3
Understand the requirement of equivalence
Before invalidating the evidence (sannad), you must establish equivalence (tasāwī) between the evidence and the negation of the objected premise. The passage says: 'after establishing equivalence, i.e., after showing that the evidence is equivalent to the non-existence of the objected premise, such that whenever the evidence is true, the non-existence of the objected premise is true, and vice versa.'
This ensures that invalidating the evidence directly refutes the objection.
4
Apply the procedure to the example
The mutakallim must prove that the philosopher's statement 'it is possible that some changing thing is eternal' is logically equivalent to 'not everything changing is originated.' If he can show this equivalence and then invalidate the philosopher's statement (e.g., by proving it is false), the objection collapses.
If the objection were without evidence, the mutakallim would simply defend the premise directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أقام الدليل ادعى تمنع مقدمة معينة منه مع السند أو مجردًا عنه. فإذا أقام المدعى الدليل ويسمى حينئذ معللا، وتمنع مقدمة معينة منه مع السند كما إذا منع الحكيم كبرى دليل المتكلم بأن يقول: لا نسلم أن كل متغير حادث مستندا بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض المتغير قديما، أو مجردا عنه (أي عاريا عن السند) فيجاب بإبطال السند إذا منع مع السند بعد إثبات التساوي، أي بعد بيان كون السند مساويا لعدم المقدمة الممنوعة بأن يكون كلما صدق السند صدق عدم المقدمة الممنوعة وبالعكس، ليفيد إبطاله بطلان المنع كأن يثبت المتكلم كون قوله: يجوز أن يكون بعض المتغير قديما، مساويا لعدم كون كل متغير حادثا.
Then when he presents the proof, the claimant objects to a specific premise from it with evidence or without it. Then when the claimant presents the proof, he is then called a reason-giver, and a specific premise from it is objected to with evidence, as when the philosopher objects to the major premise of the mutakallim's proof by saying: We do not accept that everything changing is originated, on the basis that it is not impossible that some changing thing is eternal, or without evidence (i.e., devoid of evidence). So he is answered by invalidating the evidence if the objection is with evidence, after establishing equivalence, i.e., after showing that the evidence is equivalent to the non-existence of the objected premise, such that whenever the evidence is true, the non-existence of the objected premise is true, and vice versa, so that its invalidation negates the objection, for example, the mutakallim proves that his statement 'it is possible that some changing thing is eternal' is equivalent to the non-existence of 'everything changing is originated'.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical procedure after a proof (دليل) is presented by the claimant (المدعى), who is now called the reason-giver (المعلل). The opponent may object to a specific premise of the proof in two ways: (1) with a supporting evidence (سند) for the objection, or (2) without evidence. The commentator illustrates with the earlier example: the mutakallim argues that the world is originated because it is changing, and everything changing is originated. The philosopher objects to the major premise 'everything changing is originated' by offering a reason: 'it is not impossible that some changing thing is eternal.' This objection comes with evidence (the possibility argument). The response to such an objection is to invalidate that evidence (إبطال السند). However, this invalidation requires first establishing equivalence (إثبات التساوي) between the evidence and the negation of the objected premise. That is, one must show that the truth of the evidence implies the truth of the premise's negation, and vice versa. In the example, the mutakallim must prove that the philosopher's statement 'it is possible that some changing thing is eternal' is equivalent to the negation of 'everything changing is originated.' If the evidence is invalidated, the objection collapses. If the objection was without evidence, the responder would answer directly by showing the premise's validity. This procedure ensures that objections are not arbitrary and that refutations are rigorous.

Key Terms
السند
إبطال السند
إثبات التساوي
المعلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, an objection with evidence is refuted not by attacking the objection directly but by invalidating the evidence after proving it is equivalent to the premise's negation.

Responding to Claims: Prompt or Prove
Author explains the mu'allil's dialectical obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a badihi khafi claim and a nazari majhul claim, and determine whether to respond with tanbih or dalil.

Core Idea
In dialectic, not all claims demand proof; some need only uncovering.

In a dialectical exchange (munazara), the mu'allil (claimant) must know what kind of claim he is making. If it's a hidden intuitive claim, he prompts; if it's an unknown theoretical, he proves. This distinction prevents wasting time on proofs for self-evident matters, and ensures rigor for uncertain ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim type
Determine whether the claim is intuitive (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). Intuitive claims are self-evident; theoretical ones require reasoning.
Example: 'You exist' is intuitive; 'The world is temporally originated' is theoretical.
2
Check for hiddenness or unknownness
If the claim is intuitive but not immediately obvious to the listener, it is badihi khafi. If theoretical and not known, it is nazari majhul. Only then does the rule apply.
The source says: 'the intuitive is qualified as hidden' and 'the theoretical as unknown'.
3
Select the appropriate response
For badihi khafi, provide a tanbih (prompt) to make it obvious. For nazari majhul, provide a dalil (proof, usually a syllogism).
Tanbih is not a full proof; it's a reminder.
4
Apply to the examples
Study the two examples given: the ahl al-haqq claim and the mutakallim claim. Note how the response matches the claim type.
5
Practice by constructing responses
Given a claim, decide whether to prompt or prove, and then formulate the response accordingly.
Use the apply_the_tool tasks.

وبالتنبيه أو الدليل إن ادعى بديهيا خفيًّا، أَو نَظَرِيَّا مَجْهُولًا، فَإذا أَقام الدليل ادَّعَى تُمْنَعُ مُقدِّمةٌ مُعيَّةٌ مِنْهُ مَعَ السَّنَدِ، أَوْ مُجَرَّدًا عَنْهُ. بديهيا خفيا أو نظريا مجهولا، أي يؤاخذ بالتنبيه إن ادعى بديهيا خفيا، كما إذا قال: أهل الحق، حقيقة من حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، فيقول السوفطائي: بأي تنبيه تقول؟ فيقول: لأنا نشاهد المشاهدات فلو لم تكن ثابتة لما نشاهدها، أو لأنك حقيقة من الحقائق، فلو لم تكن ثابتا لما تطلب منا التنبيه. ويؤاخذ بالدليل إن ادعى نظريا مجهولا، كما إذا قال المتكلم: العالم حادث، يقول الحكيم: بأي دليل تقول ذلك؟ فيقول: لأنه متغير، وكل متغير حادث، فهو حادث. ووجه تقييد البديهي بكونه خفيا، والنظري بكونه مجهولا لا يخفى.
And by prompting or proof if he claims an intuitive but hidden [claim] or a theoretical unknown [claim]. So when he presents the proof, he claims [something], then a specific premise from it is blocked, either with a support or without it. [He is] held to account by prompting if he claims an intuitive but hidden [claim], as when one says: 'The people of truth, a reality from the realities of things, is established.' Then the sophist says: 'By what prompting do you say?' So he says: 'Because we observe observed things; if they were not established, we would not observe them.' Or because you are a reality from realities; if you were not established, you would not ask us for prompting. And [he is] held to account by proof if he claims a theoretical unknown [claim], as when the mutakallim says: 'The world is temporally originated.' The philosopher says: 'By what proof do you say that?' So he says: 'Because it changes, and everything that changes is temporally originated, so it is temporally originated.' And the reason for qualifying the intuitive as hidden and the theoretical as unknown is not hidden.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines the initial duty of the mu'allil (the one who brings a claim) in a dialectical exchange. It distinguishes two types of claims: (1) badihi khafi (an intuitive but hidden claim) and (2) nazari majhul (a theoretical unknown claim). For the first, the mu'allil must provide a tanbih (prompting) to make the hidden obvious; for the second, he must provide a dalil (proof). The author illustrates with two examples: the first is a claim by 'ahl al-haqq' (the people of truth) about the reality of things, to which a sophist objects; the mu'allil responds by pointing to the act of observation itself as a prompting. The second is the mutakallim's claim that the world is originated (hadith), to which a philosopher demands a proof; the mu'allil offers a syllogism: the world changes, and everything that changes is originated. The author notes that the intuitive is qualified as 'hidden' because, though known, it may have some obscurity requiring clarification; the theoretical is qualified as 'unknown' because if it were not unknown, no proof would be requested.

Identifying a Blocked Premise with Sanad in Jadal
Author explains how a premise may be blocked with or without a sanad, giving an example from a philosopher-mutakallim debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a blocked premise (man' al-muqaddima) with a support (sanad) in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
A blocked premise without support is weak; a sanad gives the block force, but also opens a path to refutation.

In the dialectical tradition of jadal, when a mu'allil presents a proof, the sa'il may block a specific premise. This block can be accompanied by a support (sanad) or not. Understanding the sanad's role is crucial for the mu'allil's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's proof and the premise blocked
Find the original argument and pinpoint which premise the sa'il is objecting to.
The premise is usually marked by a phrase like 'لا نسلم' (we do not accept).
2
Locate the sanad (support) for the block
Look for a reason or justification given by the sa'il after blocking. This is the sanad.
The sanad often follows 'مستندا بأن' or similar.
3
Distinguish block with sanad from block without sanad
A block may be bare (without sanad) or supported. The mu'allil's response depends on this.
In the passage, the philosopher's block includes a sanad: 'it is permissible that some changing thing is eternal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أقام المدعى الدليل ويسمى حينئذ معللا وتمنع مقدمة معينة منه مع السند كما إذا منع الحكيم كبرى دليل المتكلم بأن يقول: لا نسلم أن كل متغير حادث مستندا بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض المتغير قديما، أو مجردا عنه أي عاريا عن السند. فيجاب بإبطال السند إذا منع مع السند بعد إثبات التساوي أي بعد بيان كون السند مساويا لعدم المقدمة الممنوعة بأن يكون كلما صدق السند صدق عدم المقدمة الممنوعة وبالعكس، ليفيد إبطاله بطلان المنع كأن يثبت المتكلم كون قوله: يجوز أن يكون بعض المتغير قديما، مساويا لعدم كون كل متغير حادثا، ثم يبطل بالدليل ذلك الجواز. أو يجاب بإثبات المقدمة الممنوعة أعم من أن لم يكن المانع مستندا بشيء أو يكون مستندا بالسند المساوي أو غيره مع التعرض بما تمسك به إن كان متمسكا بشيء، والتعرض مستحسن وليس بواجب، إذ تتم المناقشة بإثبات المقدمة بدون التعرض أيضا وهو المقصود. وقال المصنف فيما نقل عنه: إبطال السند المساوي معتبر سواء كان مساواته بحسب نفس الأمر أو بزعم المانع، لإفادته إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة تحقيقا أو تقديرا. تم كلامه.
So when the claimant presents the proof—and he is then called the mu'allil—and a specific premise from it is blocked either with a support, as when the philosopher blocks the major premise of the mutakallim's proof by saying: 'We do not accept that everything that changes is temporally originated,' supported by the fact that 'why may it not be permissible that some changing thing is eternal?' or [blocked] without it, i.e., bare of support. So he responds by refuting the support if it is blocked with a support, after establishing equivalence, i.e., after clarifying that the support is equivalent to the non-existence of the blocked premise, such that whenever the support is true, the non-existence of the blocked premise is true, and vice versa, so that its refutation yields the nullification of the blocking—as when the mutakallim establishes that his statement 'it is permissible that some changing thing is eternal' is equivalent to the non-existence of 'everything that changes is temporally originated', then he refutes that permissibility by proof. Or he responds by proving the blocked premise, whether the blocker had no support or had support that is equivalent or otherwise, along with addressing what he clung to if he clung to something—and addressing is commendable, not obligatory, for the discussion is completed by proving the premise without addressing as well, and that is the goal. And the author said in what is transmitted from him: 'Refuting the equivalent support is considered valid whether its equivalence is in reality or according to the blocker's claim, because it yields the proof of the blocked premise in fact or hypothetically.' His speech ends here.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage explains the mu'allil's procedural options when the sa'il blocks a premise (man' al-muqaddima). If the sa'il blocks a premise with a sanad (support), the mu'allil has two possible responses: (1) Refute the sanad (ibtal al-sanad) after establishing that the sanad is equivalent (musawi) to the negation of the blocked premise—meaning that if the sanad is true, then the blocked premise is false, and if the sanad is false, the blocked premise is true. Refuting the sanad then effectively proves the original premise. The author gives an example: the philosopher blocks the mutakallim's major premise 'everything that changes is temporally originated' with the sanad 'it is permissible that some changing thing is eternal'. The mutakallim must first show that this sanad is equivalent to the denial of his premise, then refute that permissibility. (2) The second option is to directly prove the blocked premise (ithbat al-muqaddima al-mamnu'a) without necessarily addressing the sanad, though addressing it is recommended. The author then cites a ruling: the equivalence of the sanad is valid whether it is real or merely according to the blocker's claim, because then refuting it hypothetically suffices to prove the premise hypothetically.

سند
إبطال السند
تساوي
إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة

A sanad is the sa'il's explicit justification for blocking a premise, and it must be identified before choosing a response.

Two Responses to a Blocked Premise: Refuting the Sanad or Proving the Premise
Author explains the mu'allil's two options when a premise is blocked with a sanad: refuting the sanad after establishing equivalence, or proving the premise directly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the mu'allil's two options—refuting the sanad after establishing equivalence, or directly proving the blocked premise—and apply the correct strategy in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Refuting the sanad works only if the sanad is equivalent to the negation of the premise; otherwise, prove the premise directly.

When the sa'il blocks a premise with a sanad, the mu'allil has two possible responses. The first involves refuting the sanad after demonstrating its equivalence to the denial of the premise. The second is simply proving the premise itself, optionally addressing the sanad. This lesson unpacks the conditions and merits of each option.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the block has a sanad
If the block is bare (no sanad), only the second option (prove the premise) is relevant. If there is a sanad, proceed to step 2.
The passage mentions block 'مع السند' (with support) or 'مجردا عنه' (bare).
2
Check equivalence (tasawi) between sanad and negation of the premise
Establish whether the sanad implies the negation of the blocked premise and vice versa. This is a logical biconditional.
In the example, 'it is permissible that some changing thing is eternal' is equivalent to 'not every changing thing is temporally originated'.
3
Choose option 1: Refute the sanad (ibtal al-sanad)
If equivalence holds, refuting the sanad (proving it false) also proves the blocked premise, because the block's support is removed.
The mu'allil must first establish equivalence, then provide a proof against the sanad.
4
Choose option 2: Prove the blocked premise directly (ithbat al-muqaddima al-mamnu'a)
If equivalence does not hold, or if preferred, the mu'allil can provide a direct proof of the blocked premise. Addressing the sanad is commendable but not obligatory.
The passage says 'التعرض مستحسن وليس بواجب' (addressing is commendable, not obligatory).
5
Note on types of equivalence
The equivalence can be real (in reality) or according to the blocker's claim, because refuting it hypothetically suffices to prove the premise hypothetically.
Quoted from the author: 'سواء كان مساواته بحسب نفس الأمر أو بزعم المانع'.

Two Methods of Responding to an Objection with a Sanad
Explains two methods of responding to an objector with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two methods of responding to an objector who supports his challenge with evidence (sanad) in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Invalidating evidence only works if it is equivalent to the premise; otherwise prove the premise directly.

In dialectical debate, when an objector challenges one of your premises by presenting evidence (sanad), you have two options: you can attack that evidence, but only if it is logically equivalent to your premise, or you can simply prove your premise directly. This lesson unpacks both methods and the condition of equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debate situation
Recognize that the objector (sā'il) has challenged a premise (al-muqaddima al-mamnū'a) by offering a counter-evidence (sanad). The arguer (mu'allil) must now choose a response.
The objector's evidence may be equivalent (musāwin) or more specific (akhass) relative to the premise.
2
Understand the first method: invalidate the evidence (ibṭāl al-sanad)
The arguer refutes the objector's evidence, but this is only effective if the evidence is equivalent to the premise—meaning if the evidence is false, the premise becomes true. If the evidence is more specific, its invalidation does not affect the premise.
Equality (tasāwī) can be real or assumed by the objector.
3
Understand the second method: prove the premise directly
The arguer simply establishes the truth of the challenged premise, regardless of whether the objector had evidence. This fulfills the primary goal of the debate.
Engagement with the objector's evidence is commendable but not obligatory.
4
Distinguish between objection (man') and invalidation (ibṭāl)
Objection is when the arguer demands proof from the objector for his evidence. This is futile because the arguer's duty is to prove his premise, not to challenge the evidence's support. Invalidation is the proper response, but only when the evidence is equivalent.
The author specifies invalidation as the correct method, not objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيُجَابُ بِابْطَالِ السَّند بعد إثباتِ التَّسَاوِي . أو بإثباتِ المُقدِّمة الممنوعة مع التّعرُّض بِما تَمَسَّكَ بِهِ . كل متغير حادثا ، ثم يبطل بالدليل ذلك الجواز ( أو ) يجاب و باثبات المقدمة الممنوعة ( أعم من أن لم يكن المانع مستندا بشيء أو يكون مستندا بالسند المساوي أو غيره ( مع التعرض بما تمسك به ) إن كان متمسكا بشيء، والتعرض الـ مستحسن وليس بواجب ، إذ تتم المناقشة بإثبات المقدمة بدون التعرض أيضا وهو المقصود ، وقال المصنف فيما نقل عنه : إبطال السند المساوي معتبر سواء كان مساواته بحسب نفس الأمر أو بزعم المانع ، لإفادته إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة تحقيقا أو تقديرا (). تم كلامه . فعلى هذا إما أن يقيد قوله : بعد إثبات التساوي ، بما إذا لم يعتقد المانع ذلك أو يراد به كونه مثبتا في ذهن السامع المانع، إما بإثبات المدعى ، أو باعتبار ظنه ثم اعلم أن دفع السند يكون على وجهين أحدهما المنع بأن يكون نظريا، فيطلب المعلل الدليل من المانع عليه وهذا عبث، لأن اللازم عليه إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة ، وإثبات السند لا ينفعه ، بل يضره . فلذا خص - قدس سره - الإبطال بالذكر والثاني الإبطال، وهو إنما ينفع إذا كان مساويا للمنع، لأن انتفاء أحد المتساويين في الخارج يدل على انتفاء الآخر فيه ، بخلاف ما إذا كان أخص فإنه لا ينفع، فإن انتفاء الأخص لا يستلزم انتفاء الأعم، والسند الأعم فهو بالحقيقة لیس بسند، ولذلك قيد المصنف الإبطال بقوله : بعد إثبات التساوي .
It is answered by invalidating the evidence after establishing equality, or by proving the challenged premise with engagement with what he used as evidence. Every changing thing is originated, then that possibility is invalidated by evidence. Or it is answered by proving the challenged premise (whether the objector had no evidence or had evidence that is equivalent or otherwise) with engagement with what he used if he used something, and engagement is commendable but not obligatory, for the debate is completed by proving the premise even without engagement, and that is the purpose. The author said in what is narrated from him: Invalidation of the equivalent evidence is considered, whether its equivalence is according to reality or according to the objector's claim, because it results in proving the challenged premise in actuality or hypothetically. His speech ends. So upon this, either his statement 'after establishing equality' is restricted to when the objector does not believe that, or it is meant as being established in the mind of the listener objector, either by proving the claim or by considering his assumption. Then know that repelling the evidence is in two ways: one is objection (man') if it is theoretical, so the arguer asks the objector for evidence for it, and this is futile, because the necessary thing for him is to prove the challenged premise, and proving the evidence does not benefit him but rather harms him. Hence the author—may his secret be sanctified—specified invalidation by mention. The second is invalidation, and it only benefits if it is equivalent to the objection, because the non-existence of one of the two equivalents in external reality indicates the non-existence of the other in it, unlike if it is more specific, for then it does not benefit, because the non-existence of the more specific does not necessitate the non-existence of the more general, and the more general evidence is in reality not evidence. Therefore the author restricted invalidation by his statement 'after establishing equality'.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains the two methods by which an arguer (mu'allil) can respond when the objector (sā'il) challenges one of his premises by offering a counter-evidence (sanad). The first method is to invalidate that evidence, but this is only effective if the evidence is equivalent (musāwin) to the premise—meaning that if the evidence is false, the premise becomes true. The second method is to directly prove the challenged premise, whether or not the objector had evidence, and this is the primary goal. The commentary elaborates that invalidating the evidence requires establishing equality (tasāwī) between the evidence and the premise, and this equality may be real or merely assumed by the objector. Then it discusses the two types of repelling evidence: one is 'man'' (objection) which is futile because the arguer's duty is to prove his premise, not to request proof of the evidence; the other is 'ibṭāl' (invalidation), which only works if the evidence is equivalent, not if it is more specific. The author's restriction 'after establishing equality' is explained as a safeguard. In Islamic legal dialectic, this is akin to a debater saying 'Your evidence is invalid because it implies a contradiction', but only if that evidence is co-extensive with the claim.

إبطال السند
المقدمة الممنوعة
التساوي
منع
دفع السند

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when an objector challenges a premise by presenting evidence (sanad), what condition must be met for the arguer to effectively invalidate that evidence?
A) The evidence must be from the Qur'an or Sunnah.
B) The evidence must be equivalent (musāwin) to the premise.
C) The evidence must be more specific (akhass).
D) The evidence must be accepted by both parties.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debate, invalidating an objector's evidence is only effective if it is logically equivalent to the challenged premise; otherwise, directly proving the premise is the proper response.

Responding to Naqḍ and Muʿāraḍa in Dialectical Debate
Explains how the arguer replies when the objector uses naqḍ or muʿāraḍa against his proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to choose the appropriate response (manʿ, naqḍ, muʿāraḍa, or taghyīr/taḥrīr) when an objector attacks your proof using naqḍ or muʿāraḍa in a jadal context.

Core Idea
In dialectic, every objection has a counter: object, counter-object, or modify your proof.

In jadal (dialectical debate), when an opponent attacks your proof with naqḍ (counterexample or absurdity) or muʿāraḍa (parallel argument), you are not left defenseless. This lesson from the classical tradition equips you with the exact responses allowed: you can object to the objection itself (manʿ), counter with your own naqḍ or muʿāraḍa, or refine your proof. Understanding these options is crucial for any structured theological or juristic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of attack
Determine if the objector used naqḍ (specifying which subtype: takhalluf or luzūm al-muḥāl) or muʿāraḍa (qalb, mithl, or ghayr).
The objector's method constrains your response options.
2
Assess the validity of the attack
Check whether the objection genuinely challenges your proof. For naqḍ, ensure it is a real counterexample or truly leads to absurdity. For muʿāraḍa, verify it is a genuine parallel.
If the objection is invalid, you can simply dismiss it with manʿ.
3
Choose a response
You have three main responses: manʿ (deny a premise of the objection), counter-naqḍ or counter-muʿāraḍa (turn the opponent's method against him), or taghyīr/taḥrīr (modify or clarify your proof to avoid the objection).
Taghyīr and taḥrīr are available in all cases (fī al-kulli muṭlaqan).
4
Verify the condition for ibṭāl (invalidation)
If you choose to invalidate the opponent's evidence, you must first establish equivalence (musāwāh) between your evidence and his counter-evidence. Invalidation only works if the two are equal in scope; otherwise, it fails.
The text warns that invalidation without equivalence is futile.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is objected to by one of the two ways and it is countered by one of the three ways. Then it is answered by objection, or by counter-objection, or by counter-argument, and by modification or clarification in all absolutely. The arguer (demands) evidence from the objector for it, and this is futile, because the necessary thing for him is to prove the challenged premise, and proving the evidence does not benefit him but rather harms him. Hence the author—may his secret be sanctified—specified invalidation by mention. The second is invalidation, and it only benefits if it is equivalent to the objection, because the non-existence of one of the two equivalents in external reality indicates the non-existence of the other in it, unlike if it is more specific, for then it does not benefit, because the non-existence of the more specific does not necessitate the non-existence of the more general, and the more general evidence is in reality not evidence. Then it is answered by objection, or by counter-objection, or by counter-argument, and by modification or clarification in all absolutely.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage describes the arsenal of responses available to an arguer when the objector employs naqḍ (showing the conclusion fails or leads to absurdity) or mu'āraḍa (offering a parallel argument). The text begins by stating that the objector can use either of two methods for naqḍ—takhalluf (the proof does not entail the conclusion in a certain case) or luzūm al-muḥāl (the proof would force a contradiction such as two opposites co-existing). For mu'āraḍa, the objector has three methods: qalb (inversion, flipping the premise), mithl (an analogous proof), or ghayr (a different type of counter-argument). In response, the arguer can either simply object (man‘) to the premises of the naqḍ/mu'āraḍa, or counter with another naqḍ or mu'āraḍa of his own. Additionally, in all cases, the arguer may modify (taghyīr) his original proof or clarify (taḥrīr) its meaning. The commentary reiterates the earlier point about invalidation of evidence being restricted to equivalence, though this is a tangential insertion. In a typical Islamic juristic debate, if a mutakallim argues 'The world is created because it is changeable', and the objector says 'But what about accidents? They change yet might be eternal' (naqḍ by takhalluf), the arguer could respond by objecting to the objector's example or by modifying his definition of change.

Key Takeaway
The arguer in jadal has a versatile toolkit: he can object to the objection, counter with his own attack, or refine his proof, depending on the type of assault.

The Three Attacks in Jadal: Prevention, Refutation, Counter-argument
Explains the three attacks and the responder's possible responses including taghyir and tahrir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three dialectical attacks (man', naqd, mu'arada) is being used in a given debate exchange and explain its purpose.

Core Idea
In jadal, every proof faces three possible attacks: prevention denies a premise, refutation exposes a flaw, counter-argument offers a competing proof.

The art of jadal (dialectics) relies on a structured exchange between a questioner (sā'il) and a responder (mu'allil). The questioner has three standard weapons: prevention (man‘), refutation (naqd), and counter-argument (mu‘āraḍa). Understanding each attack is crucial for both defending a position and breaking down an opponent's proof.

Guided Path
1
Define man‘ (prevention)
The questioner denies a specific premise of the responder's proof, forcing the responder to prove that premise. This is the most basic attack.
In the passage, prevention is discussed as a position (manṣib) of the questioner.
2
Define naqd (refutation)
The questioner shows that the proof fails either because the conclusion does not follow (takhalluf) or because it leads to an impossibility (luzūm al-muḥāl).
Naqd can be applied directly to the proof, not just to a premise.
3
Define mu‘āraḍa (counter-argument)
The questioner presents a parallel proof that conflicts with the responder's proof. It has three subtypes: qalb (inversion, same form and matter), mithl (similar, same form but different matter), and ghayr (other, different both).
The passage mentions these three aspects.
4
Recognise the responder's first line of answer
The responder may answer with man‘, naqd, or mu‘āraḍa, depending on which attack is used. Additionally, he may change (taghyīr) or reformulate (taḥrīr) his claim to avoid the attack.
The word 'or' (aw) in the text indicates that the responder can choose any one, not all simultaneously.

Source Text

The mu'allil (the one providing the proof) [seeks] the proof from the one who prevents, and this is futile, because what is required of him is to prove the prevented premise, and proving the proof (sanad) does not benefit him, rather it harms him. Therefore he (quddisa sirruhu) specified invalidation (ibṭāl) by mention, and the second is invalidation, and it only benefits if it is equal (musāwī) to the prevention, because the non-existence of one of two equal things in the external world indicates the non-existence of the other in it, unlike the case when it is more specific (akhaṣ), for then it does not benefit, because the non-existence of the more specific does not entail the non-existence of the more general (aʿamm), and the proof that is more general is in reality not a proof, and therefore the author restricted invalidation by his saying: after establishing equality. Third, from the questioner's positions is counter-argument (muʿāraḍa), and it is either by inversion (qalb) when the proof of the mu'allil and the questioner are united in matter and form with their indications, or by similar (mithl) when they are united in form only with a difference in indication, or by other (ghayr) which is when they differ. In the two cases of refutation and counter-argument, [it is answered] by prevention if it is susceptible to it, or refutation if it is suitable for it, or counter-argument if it is susceptible to it, because the first mu'allil — after refutation and counter-argument — becomes a questioner, so he has three positions just as the first questioner had, and he may direct the three questions to each one of them, so the word 'or' (aw) is for preventing vacancy (manʿ al-khalaw) not conjunction (jamʿ). (Footnote 1) We have said that the questioner has three positions: prevention (manʿ), refutation (naqḍ), and counter-argument (muʿāraḍa). And the mu'allil may also have these three positions in the two cases of refutation and counter-argument from the questioner. So he can prevent a premise from the premises of the questioner's proof by asking for its proof. And he can invalidate the questioner's proof, and he can counter-argue it with the types of counter-argument: by inversion, by similar, or by other. And he also has other positions besides these three: he can change the claim or reformulate it absolutely, i.e., in the case of prevention, refutation, or counter-argument. And he can also combine prevention, refutation, and counter-argument. Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical exchanges in jadal, focusing on the three standard attacks a questioner (sā'il) can make against a proof (dalīl) presented by the responder (mu'allil), and the responder's possible replies. The text first states that the proof can be refuted (naqḍ) in one of two ways and countered (muʿāraḍa) in one of three ways. The responder then answers with one of three responses: prevention (manʿ), refutation (naqḍ), or counter-argument (muʿāraḍa), and additionally has the option to change (taghyīr) or reformulate (taḥrīr) in all cases. The author then delves into the discussion of invalidation (ibṭāl). If the questioner prevents a premise by providing a proof (sanad) that it is false, the responder has two options: either to ask for a proof for that premise (which is futile because his obligation is to prove his own premise, not to attack the proof), or to invalidate the proof if it is equal in scope (musāwī) to his prevention. If the proof is more specific (akhaṣ), invalidating it does not help because the non-existence of the specific does not entail the non-existence of the general. The author then details the two aspects of refutation: (1) failure of the proof (the proof exists but its conclusion does not), and (2) necessitation of an impossibility (e.g., contradiction). Next, he describes the three types of counter-argument: inversion (when both proofs are identical in matter and form), similar (same form but different matter), and other (different in both). The text then transitions to the alert (tanbīh), which is a statement of a self-evident truth. Attacks against it are possible but ineffective because the claim it supports needs no proof. The responder's role after being attacked changes: he becomes the questioner with the same three positions. Finally, the author notes that 'or' in 'by prevention, or refutation, or counter-argument' indicates that the responder may use any one of these, but not necessarily all; it prevents vacancy (i.e., he must answer somehow), not conjunction (he is not required to use all). The responder may also change or reformulate his claim. Throughout, the examples are drawn from logical and legal debates, such as the condition of equality for invalidation to be effective.

معارضة
تغيير
تحرير

Key Takeaway
The three attacks — prevention, refutation, and counter-argument — are the core tools of the questioner in jadal; the responder can meet them with the same three responses plus the options of changing or reformulating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the attack used in this exchange: Responder: 'The world is created because it changes.' Questioner: 'I deny that the world changes; prove it.' Answer: This is man‘ (prevention) because the questioner denies a premise.
?
In a debate about the prohibition of riba, the questioner says: 'Your proof that all loans with interest are forbidden fails because if that were the case, then zero-interest loans would also be forbidden, which they are not.' Which attack is this? Answer: This is naqd (refutation by takhalluf — the conclusion does not follow).

Invalidation (Ibṭāl) and the Condition of Equality
Focuses on the logic of invalidation (ibṭāl) and the necessity of equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when invalidating the questioner's proof (ibṭāl) is a valid response by applying the equality (musāwī) condition.

Core Idea
Invalidating the questioner's proof only helps if it equals the scope of the prevention; otherwise it is useless.

When a questioner prevents a premise by providing a proof (sanad) to invalidate it, the responder has two options: demand proof for that premise, or invalidate the questioner's proof. However, the latter only works if the questioner's proof is exactly equal in scope to the premise it attacks. This condition of equality (musāwī) is crucial for effective dialectical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the situation: the questioner brings a proof (sanad) to invalidate a premise
The questioner does not just deny a premise; he supports his denial with a proof that the premise is false.
This is a stronger form of man‘, called man‘ ma‘a al-sanad.
2
Recognise the two possible responses
The responder can either (a) ask the questioner to prove that premise (which is futile because the responder should prove his own premise) or (b) invalidate the questioner's proof (ibṭāl).
As the commentary says, asking for proof is 'futile' (ʿabath).
3
Apply the equality condition for ibṭāl
Ibṭāl only benefits the responder if the questioner's proof (sanad) is equal in scope (musāwī) to the prevented premise. If the proof is more specific (akhaṣ), invalidating it does not entail invalidation of the prevented premise; the non-existence of a specific does not imply the non-existence of the general.
Example: if the premise is 'All wine is intoxicating' and the proof is 'Only red wine is intoxicating' (more specific), disproving that proof doesn't disprove the original premise.
4
Distinguish equality from specificity
The passage explains that if two things are equal in extension, the non-existence of one implies the non-existence of the other. But if one is more general (aʿamm) and the other more specific (akhaṣ), the non-existence of the specific does not imply the non-existence of the general.
This mirrors logical conditions in concept containment.

And the mu'allil may also have these three positions in the two cases of refutation and counter-argument from the questioner. So he can prevent a premise from the premises of the questioner's proof by asking for its proof. And he also has other positions besides these three: he can change the claim or reformulate it absolutely, i.e., in the case of prevention, refutation, or counter-argument. Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical exchanges in jadal, focusing on the three standard attacks a questioner (sā'il) can make against a proof (dalīl) presented by the responder (mu'allil), and the responder's possible replies. The text first states that the proof can be refuted (naqḍ) in one of two ways and countered (muʿāraḍa) in one of three ways. The responder then answers with one of three responses: prevention (manʿ), refutation (naqḍ), or counter-argument (muʿāraḍa), and additionally has the option to change (taghyīr) or reformulate (taḥrīr) in all cases. The author then delves into the discussion of invalidation (ibṭāl). If the questioner prevents a premise by providing a proof (sanad) that it is false, the responder has two options: either to ask for a proof for that premise (which is futile because his obligation is to prove his own premise, not to attack the proof), or to invalidate the proof if it is equal in scope (musāwī) to his prevention. If the proof is more specific (akhaṣ), invalidating it does not help because the non-existence of the specific does not entail the non-existence of the general. The author then details the two aspects of refutation: (1) failure of the proof (the proof exists but its conclusion does not), and (2) necessitation of an impossibility (e.g., contradiction). Next, he describes the three types of counter-argument: inversion (when both proofs are identical in matter and form), similar (same form but different matter), and other (different in both). The text then transitions to the alert (tanbīh), which is a statement of a self-evident truth. Attacks against it are possible but ineffective because the claim it supports needs no proof. The responder's role after being attacked changes: he becomes the questioner with the same three positions. Finally, the author notes that 'or' in 'by prevention, or refutation, or counter-argument' indicates that the responder may use any one of these, but not necessarily all; it prevents vacancy (i.e., he must answer somehow), not conjunction (he is not required to use all). The responder may also change or reformulate his claim. Throughout, the examples are drawn from logical and legal debates, such as the condition of equality for invalidation to be effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate: Responder: 'Riba is forbidden because it involves exploitation.' Questioner prevents the premise 'All transactions with exploitation are forbidden' and provides a proof: 'Some exploitative sales (e.g., inflated pricing) are permitted.' Is this proof equal to the prevented premise? If not, what should the responder do? The questioner prevents 'Allah's speech is uncreated' with a proof: 'The word of Allah as revealed to Musa (as) was a created sound.' Is this proof equal? If the responder invalidates it, does it help? Explain.

The Three Objections Apply to Tanbih with Lesser Effect
Explains that tanbih is subject to man', naqd, and mu'arada but with less effect than istidlal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of the three dialectical objections on tanbih versus istidlal, and describe how the roles of mu'allil and sa'il shift during an objection.

Core Idea
Tanbih is subject to objections, but its self-evident claim withstands them unlike istidlal.

In dialectic (jadal), objections come in three forms: man', naqd, and mu'arada. You already know these apply to istidlal (inferential proof). But what about tanbih (notification)? This passage from the Rashidiyya explores whether tanbih is also vulnerable, and how its purpose—removing obscurity rather than proving a claim—changes the stakes. We'll walk through the author's reasoning and see the mechanics of who becomes the questioner and respondent.

Guided Path
1
Contrast tanbih and istidlal
Identify the core difference in purpose: tanbih aims to remove obscurity (إزالة الخفاء), while istidlal aims to prove the claim (إثبات الدعوى). Understand that because tanbih does not aim to prove, objections do not undermine the claim itself.
2
Recognize the three objections
List man' (denial), naqd (breach), and mu'arada (opposition). Note that the word 'aw (or) indicates they are alternatives but can be combined (man'at al-khuluw, not man'at al-jam').
3
Trace the role reversal
In responses to naqd and mu'arada, the mu'allil (proof-giver) becomes a questioner (sa'il) and gains the same three positions: he can deny, breach, or oppose the opponent's argument. Understand how this dynamic works.
4
Evaluate the impact on tanbih
The commentator notes that objecting to tanbih does not damage the claim because the claim is self-evident and independent of proof. The loss of removing obscurity is minimal. However, the author hints at a weakness with 'fa-tammal' (so reflect). Consider why the removal of obscurity might still matter.
5
Synthesize the dialectical rule
For any tanbih, the sa'il can object, but the mu'allil can respond by changing (taghyir) or reformulating (tahrir) the claim to avoid the objection. This response is permissible in all cases, whether the sa'il used man', naqd, or mu'arada.

Source Text

وأمَّا التنبيه فَيَتَوَجَّهُ عليهِ ذلكَ ، وَلَا يَكثرُ نَفْعُهُ إذْ لَم يُقصَدْ بهِ إِثْبَاتُ الدعوى ، فلا يَقْدَحُ في ثُبُوتِهِ المُسْتَغْنِي عَنِ الإِثْبَاتِ بِخِلافِ الاستدلال . في صورتي النقض والمعارضة بالمنع إذا كان قابلا له أو النقض إن كان صالحا له أو المعارضة إن كان قابلا لها، لأن المعلل الأول - بعد النقض والمعارضة - يصير سائلا ، فيكون له ثلاثة مناصب، كما كانت للسائل الأول ، وقد يورد الأسئلة الثلاثة على كل واحد منهما ، فكلمة أو لمنع الخلو دون الجمع، ويجوز الجواب بالتغيير أي بتغيير الأصل أو التحرير بحيث لا يرد عليه شيء في الكل مطلقا ، سواء كان السائل مانعا ، أو ناقضا ، أو معارضا ، وسواء كان الجواب بتغير الدعوى، أو المقدمة الممنوعة . وأما التنبيه فيتوجه عليه ذلك، أي ما ذكره من الأسئلة الثلاثة ولا يكثر نفعه أي نفع ذلك التوجه إذا لم يقصد به أي بذكر ذلك التنبيه إثبات الدعوى لكونها بديهية غير محتاجة إلى الإثبات فلا يقدح ذلك التوجه في ثبوته أي الدعوى بتأويل المطلوب أو المدعى المستغنى عن الإثبات بخلاف الاستدلال، فإن التوجه هناك يقدح في ثبوت الدعوى، لكونه محتاجا إليه ، وكان الأولى أن يذكر الدليل بدليل الاستدلال، وقد يناقش هنا بأنه دليل المعلل والسائل صورة ومادة . وقد تقدم ذكر هذا . لقد قلنا أن للسائل ثلاثة مناصب : هي المنع والنقض والمعارضة ، وقد يكون للمعلل هذه المناصب الثلاثة أيضا في صورتي النقض والمعارضة من السائل. فله أن يمنع مقدمة من مقدمات دليل السائل بأن يطلب عليها الدليل . وله أن يبطل دليل السائل، وله أن يعارضه بأنواع المعارضة بالقلب أو بالمثل أو بالغير، كما أن له مناصب أخرى غير هذه المناصب الثلاثة بأن يغير الدعوى أو يحررها مطلقا أي في حالة المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة. كما أن له أن يجمع بين المنع والنقض والمعارضة لقد عرفنا أن دليل المعلل يرد عليه إما المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة . أو ترد عليه كلها مرة واحدة . لأن أو مانعة خلو لا مانعة جمع. وهنا بين المصنف أن التنبيه يتوجه عليه أيضا المنع والنقض والمعارضة . إلا أن التوجه كما يفوت بالأسئلة المذكورة ما هو مقصود بالاستدلال - أعني إثبات المدعي - كذلك يفوت بها ما هو مقصود من التنبيه أيضا ، أعني إزالة الخفاء ، فلا فرق إلا أن يقال : إن المقصود الأصلي هو ثبوت المدعي ، وأما زوال الخفاء فقد يحصل بأدنى تأمل للسائل الطالب للحق أيضا، فلا اعتداد بفواته . ولا يخفى ما فيه . فتأمل
As for tanbih (notification), it is subject to that [the three questions], but its benefit is not great since it is not intended to prove the claim, so it does not undermine the establishment [of the claim] which is independent of proof, unlike istidlal. In the two cases of naqd and mu'arada, [the respondent can respond] with man' if it is applicable, or naqd if it is suitable, or mu'arada if it is acceptable, because the first mu'allil (the one who gives the proof), after naqd and mu'arada, becomes a questioner, so he has three positions, just as the first questioner had. He may pose the three questions to each one of them, and the word 'aw (or) is for preventing emptiness, not for preventing combination. And it is permissible to answer with taghyir (changing) i.e., changing the original, or with tahrir (reformulation) such that nothing applies to it in all cases absolutely, whether the questioner is one who denies, one who objects by breach, or one who opposes, and whether the answer is by changing the claim or the denied premise. And the tanbih is subject to that, i.e., the three questions mentioned, and its benefit is not great, i.e., the benefit of that attention, if it is not intended by it, i.e., by mentioning that tanbih, to prove the claim because it is self-evident and does not need proof. So that attention does not undermine its establishment, i.e., the claim, by interpreting the desired [claim] as independent of proof, unlike istidlal, for attention there undermines the establishment of the claim because it is in need of it. And it would have been better to mention the proof instead of istidlal. It may be discussed here that it is the proof of the mu'allil and the questioner in form and matter. This has been mentioned before. We have said that the questioner has three positions: man', naqd, and mu'arada. The mu'allil may also have these three positions in the two cases of naqd and mu'arada from the questioner. So he may deny a premise from the premises of the questioner's proof by requesting proof for it. And he may invalidate the questioner's proof, and he may oppose it with types of mu'arada: by inversion, by similarity, or by something else. He also has other positions besides these three, such as changing the claim or reformulating it absolutely, i.e., in the case of man', naqd, or mu'arada. He may also combine man', naqd, and mu'arada. We have stated that the mu'allil's proof is subject to either man', naqd, or mu'arada, or all of them at once, because 'aw is for preventing emptiness, not for preventing combination. Here the author explains that tanbih is also subject to man', naqd, and mu'arada. However, the attention [to it] is like that: just as what is intended by istidlal—namely, proving the claim—is lost by the mentioned questions, so too what is intended by tanbih—namely, removing obscurity—is lost by them. So there is no difference, except it is said: the primary purpose is the establishment of the claim, while the removal of obscurity may be achieved by the least reflection from the questioner who is a seeker of truth, so its loss is not considered. And what is in it is not hidden. So reflect.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn concerning the application of the three types of objections (man', naqd, mu'arada) to tanbih (notification/alert) as opposed to istidlal (inferential argument). The author first states that tanbih is indeed subject to these objections, but its benefit is limited because tanbih is not intended to prove the claim; rather, it merely removes obscurity. Therefore, raising objections against tanbih does not damage the establishment of the claim, which is independent of proof. In contrast, istidlal aims to prove the claim, so objections against it do damage the claim. The commentary then details the mechanics: in the cases where the questioner uses naqd or mu'arada, the respondent (al-mu'allil) can respond with man' (denial) if the objection is applicable, or with naqd (breach) if suitable, or with mu'arada (opposition) if acceptable. After the respondent deals with naqd or mu'arada, he becomes a questioner himself, thus having the same three positions as the original questioner. The word 'aw (or) used in the text indicates that these are alternatives, but they can be combined (man'at al-khuluw, not man'at al-jam'). The respondent may also answer by changing the original claim (taghyir) or reformulating (tahrir) it to avoid the objection entirely. The author then reiterates that tanbih is subject to the same three questions, but its benefit is minimal if it is not used for proof, as the claim is self-evident. The commentary adds that the questioner has three positions (man', naqd, mu'arada), and the respondent can also have these when the questioner uses naqd or mu'arada. The respondent can deny a premise, invalidate the proof, or oppose it with various types of mu'arada (by inversion, similarity, or other). He may also change the claim or reformulate it. The concluding note explains that the intended purpose of istidlal is to prove the claim, while that of tanbih is to remove obscurity. Although both are lost by objections, the loss for tanbih is not critical because removing obscurity can often be achieved with minimal reflection by a sincere questioner. The author hints at a weakness in this answer with 'fa-tammal' (so reflect).

C) Because tanbih is always self-evident and cannot be objected to.

Key Takeaway
Tanbih is vulnerable to objections, but because it does not aim to prove the claim, the objections do not refute the claim itself; the claim stands independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a real fiqh claim that is often presented as tanbih (e.g., 'Alcohol is impure') and role-play a dialectical exchange: the sa'il objects with naqd (showing a case where alcohol might be considered not impure), and the mu'allil responds by reformulating the claim. Describe whether the response is effective according to the rules here.

How Real Definitions Are Challenged in Dialectic: Man', Naqd, and Mu'arada
Explains that real definitions contain implicit claims and can be objected to by denial, breach, or opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three dialectical objections (man', naqd, mu'arada) to any real definition used in Islamic scholarly debate.

Core Idea
Every real definition makes implicit claims that can be denied, breached, or opposed.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), definitions are not taken as given; they contain implicit claims that they capture the essence. This passage explains how a real definition can be challenged by three standard objections: denying its parts, breaching its comprehensiveness, or opposing it with an alternative. Understanding these moves is essential for participating in or analyzing any scholarly debate in usul, kalam, or fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implicit claims in the definition
Recognise that every real definition claims to be a proper hadd, with the first part as genus and the second as differentia. These are the claims that can be challenged.
These are called al-da'awa al-dimniyya.
2
Apply man' (denial)
Deny that the given expression is a definition, or deny that its parts are the correct genus and differentia. This is a direct rejection of the definition's validity.
Man' is a simple refusal to accept a premise.
3
Apply naqd by tard (integrality)
Show that the definition fails to include all instances of the defined; i.e., it is too narrow. Point out a case that should be included but is not.
Tard means the definition must cover all members of the defined category.
4
Apply naqd by 'aks (co-extensiveness)
Show that the definition fails to exclude all non-instances; i.e., it is too broad. Point out a case that should not be included but is.
'Aks means the definition must not include anything outside the defined category.
5
Apply mu'arada (opposition)
Present an alternative definition that the definer acknowledges as valid. Since concepts do not conflict, the definer must accept that his definition is not the only possible one.
The alternative must be recognised by the definer for the objection to hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني ما سيتلى عليك وهو قوله : ( التعريف الحقيقي لاشتماله على دعاوى ضمنية ) وهي أن هذا المذكور حد له ، والجزء الأول جنس له ، والثاني فصل له ( يمنع ) بأن يقال : لا نسلم أنه حد له ، أو الأول جنس ، والثاني فصل ( وينقض بيان الاختلال في طرده بأن يقال : ما ذكرت ليس بمانع لدخول فرد من أفراد غير المحدود فيه وعكسه بأن يقال : ذلك ليس بجامع لخروج فرد من أفراد المحدد عنه ويعارض بغيره ) أي بحد غير ما ذكره، لكن لابد أن يكون ذلك الغير مما يعترف به الحاد ، إذ لا تعارض بين التصورات ، فإن أحدا منها لا يمنع الآخر ) . قيل : كما عليه لا ينفع السائل في القدح في المطلوب. لأنه مستغن عن الثبوت . (۱) يريد الشارح بهذا أن المقصود من التنبيه إزالة خفاء المطلوب كما أن المقصود من الدليل إثبات المطلوب . أن يتجه على التنبيه المنع والنقض والمعارضة كما تتجه على الدليل. وقد أجاب بأن المقصود الأصلي هو ثبوت المدعى . وأما زوال الخفاء فليس مقصودا. لأنه قد يحصل للطالب بأقل تأمل إذا كان السائل طالبا للحق. وإن كان قد نبه على ضعف هذا الجواب بقوله : فتأمل . (۲) يريد المصنف أن يبين كيفية توجه المنع والنقض والمعارضة على التعريف مع أنها لا ترد إلا على الدعوى
The second investigation: What will be recited to you, and it is his statement: 'The real definition, because it contains implicit claims, is subject to denial (man'), breach (naqd) by showing the defect in its integrality (tard) and its co-extensiveness ('aks), and opposition (mu'arada) with another [definition].' These [implicit claims] are that this mentioned [definition] is a definition (hadd) for it, the first part is a genus (jins) for it, and the second part is a differentia (fasl) for it. It is denied by saying: we do not accept that it is a definition for it, or that the first is a genus and the second a differentia. It is breached by showing the defect in its integrality by saying: what you mentioned is not a preventer [of entry] because an individual of the indefinite [non-defined] enters it, and [by showing the defect] in its co-extensiveness by saying: that is not a unifier because an individual of the defined exits from it. And it is opposed by another [definition] i.e., by a definition other than what he mentioned, but that other must be acknowledged by the definer, because there is no opposition between concepts, for none of them prevents the other. It was said: as it does not benefit the questioner in criticizing the desired [claim] because it is independent of proof. (1) The commentator means by this that the purpose of tanbih is to remove the obscurity of the desired claim, just as the purpose of the proof is to establish the desired claim. That man', naqd, and mu'arada apply to tanbih just as they apply to the proof. And he answered that the primary purpose is the establishment of the claim, while the removal of obscurity is not intended; because it may be achieved by the least reflection for the student if the questioner is a seeker of truth, although he alerted to the weakness of this answer by his saying: 'so reflect.' (2) The author intends to show how man', naqd, and mu'arada apply to the definition, even though they are only applied to claims.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn concerning the real definition (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi) and how it is subject to the three standard objections in dialectic. The author states that the real definition contains implicit claims (da'awa dimniyya): namely, that the given expression is a proper definition (hadd), that its parts are genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). Therefore, it can be denied (man') by rejecting that it is a definition or that the components are genus and differentia. It can be breached (naqd) by showing failure in integrality (tard): that the definition includes something it should not; or failure in co-extensiveness ('aks): that the definition excludes something it should include. It can also be opposed (mu'arada) by presenting an alternative definition, but that alternative must be acknowledged by the definer, because there is no incompatibility between mere concepts. The commentary adds a note that the questioner may not benefit from objecting to the definition if the claim is self-evident. Then two footnotes: the first explains that the purpose of tanbih is to remove obscurity, and objections apply to it as well, but the primary purpose is establishing the claim. The second note clarifies that the author is showing how these objections apply to definitions despite usually being applied to claims.

When objecting to a real definition using 'naqd', what exactly is being shown?
A) That the definition does not produce new knowledge.
B) That the definition fails in integrality (tard) or co-extensiveness ('aks).
C) That the genus is not a proper category.
D) That the definition is too long.

Key Takeaway
Real definitions are dialectical claims that can be challenged by denial, breach of comprehensiveness, or opposition with an alternative definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'wajib' as that which is rewarded for doing and punished for leaving. Apply man' by denying that this is a proper hadd; then apply naqd by showing a case where something is rewarded but not punished (e.g., recommended acts).
?
In a debate, after a definer proposes a definition of 'khabar mutawatir' as a report transmitted by a multitude precluding collusion on falsehood, oppose it (mu'arada) with an alternative definition from another scholar, ensuring it is acknowledged by the definer.
