When Does Man' (Objection) Apply? Literal vs. Metaphorical Usage
Commentary explains that man' applies to definitions metaphorically and does not truly apply to transmission and claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal and metaphorical uses of man' in dialectical debates, and identify when man' truly applies to a premise versus when it is used metaphorically for transmissions and claims.

Core Idea
Man' is a request for evidence on a premise; it cannot literally target a bare claim or a transmission.

In Islamic dialectics (munazara), the term man' refers to a specific objection: demanding evidence for a particular premise. But not every 'I do not accept' qualifies as a real man'. This lesson explores the commentary on when man' is used literally and when metaphorically, especially when objecting to definitions, transmitted statements, or initial claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technical definition of man'
Extract from the passage the precise definition: man' is a request for evidence on a specific premise in an argument (طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة).
This is the core meaning that distinguishes man' from other objections like naqd and mu'āraḍa.
2
Distinguish the three contexts: definition, transmission, claim
The passage discusses man' in relation to definitions (hadd), transmission (naql), and bare claims (da'wa). Understand that each has a different relationship with man' according to the commentator.
Definitions are not premises in an argument but the subject of debate; transmissions are citations of authorities; claims are assertions without evidence.
3
Analyse the case of definition
The commentator says applying man' to questions raised against definitions is metaphorical, though possibly literal if the term is also set for that context. Definitions themselves are not premises, so objecting to them is like objecting to the definition itself, not to a premise.
This shows that the literal vs. metaphorical distinction depends on whether the objection targets a premise in an established argument.
4
Analyse the case of transmission
When someone quotes an authority (e.g., Abu Hanifa on intention in wudu), the objector may deny the attribution. This is not a demand for evidence on a premise but a request to verify the transmission. Hence, man' is used metaphorically due to the shared aspect of being a request.
The objector cannot be demanding evidence for a premise because the transmission itself is not part of an argument; it is a citation.
5
Analyse the case of bare claim
When a speaker makes a claim like 'the body is composed of indivisible parts' without evidence, the objector's 'we do not accept' cannot be a demand for a specific premise (since no evidence exists). It is either meaningless or a request for evidence for the claim itself, which is metaphorical.
This highlights that man' presupposes an existing argument with premises.

ه
وقيل إنما الممنوع منع المنقول من حيث هو منقول ، لعدم التزام صحته )
(۱) لما كان المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة من الأدلة التي يقيمها المعلل على دعواه قال المصنف . إن المنع لا يتوجه حقيقة على النقل والدعوى وإنما يتوجه عليهما مجازا . أما النقل فإن الناقل إنما ينقل كلام غيره ولا يتحمل صحنه أو فساده فلا يقيم على المنقول أدلة . وإنما يمكن فقط أن يقول السائل . إننا لا نسلم أن يكون ذلك القول لمن نسبته إليه فيكون هذا منعا مجازا لا حقيقة . وأما الدعوى فإنها لم تشتمل على دليل حتى يصح أن يقال أطلب الدليل عليها . لكن يجوز أن يحمل هذا المنع على أن المراد منه إقامة الدليل على الدعوى وعلى ذلك يكون إطلاق المنع على مثل هذا مجازا لا حقيقة . وكذلك لا يتجه النقض والمعارضة على النقل والدعوى كما لا يتجه المنع عليهما .
And know that the application of 'al-mamnū' (the objected) there is by way of metaphor, and it may be real. The third investigation: It becomes clear from what we mentioned that the objection (man') does not truly apply to transmission (naql) and claim (da'wa), since its return to the premise is not intended. And know that the application of 'al-mamnū' – meaning man', naqd, and mu'āraḍa, and in their speech the word man' is applied to each of them – there, meaning to the questions raised against definitions, is by way of explicit metaphor, considering their resemblance to the technical terms, and it may be real, based on the fact that the mentioned words, just as they are set for the well-known meanings, may be set for those meanings as well. Thus it is transmitted from him, may his secret be sanctified. The third investigation: What becomes clear from his saying: 'becomes clear' i.e. appears – from what we mentioned that man' is requesting evidence for a specific premise – that man' does not truly apply to transmission and claim, whether they are active or passive in meaning, meaning the claimed and the transmitted, since its return, i.e. the return of man' to the premise, i.e. the premise mentioned in the evidence of the arguer, is not intended. As for transmission, because when someone says: Abu Hanifa – may Allah have mercy on him – said: intention is not a condition in ablution, then either the objector says: we do not accept that it is not a condition in it, or he says: we do not accept that Abu Hanifa said such-and-such. The first is not heard at all, because he presented the speech by way of narration, so no blame attaches to it. As for the second, even if it is heard, it is not as a real objection, but rather because it is an expression of requesting the correction of the transmission; the word man' is applied to it metaphorically due to the shared aspect that each of them is a request, by way of using a restricted word in another restricted context for its unrestricted meaning, so the word man' is used. As for the claim, because when the speaker says: the body is composed of indivisible parts, and the philosopher says: we do not accept that, then either he intends by it to request evidence for the specific premise, and this is meaningless, because there is no evidence with the claimant yet to request evidence for a specific premise from it, or he intends to request evidence for that claim, and this is heard, but it is not a real objection; rather the word man' is applied to it metaphorically, as you have understood, like naqd and mu'āraḍa – i.e. just as naqd and mu'āraḍa do not apply due to the lack of the mentioned evidence for proof. And it was said: The only thing prevented is the prevention of the transmitted, insofar as it is transmitted, due to the lack of commitment to its correctness. (1) Since man' is requesting evidence for a specific premise from the evidence that the reasoner presents for his claim, the author said: man' does not truly apply to transmission and claim; rather it applies to them metaphorically. As for transmission, the transmitter only transmits the speech of another and does not bear its correctness or corruption, so he does not present evidence for the transmitted. The questioner can only say: we do not accept that that statement is from the one to whom it is attributed, so this is a metaphorical objection, not real. As for the claim, it does not contain evidence such that it is correct to say: I request evidence for it. However, this objection may be interpreted as intending to bring evidence for the claim, and on that basis, the application of man' to such is metaphorical, not real. Likewise, naqd and mu'āraḍa do not apply to transmission and claim, just as man' does not apply to them.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concept of man' (objection) in dialectical debates, specifically addressing whether the term 'man' ' is used literally or metaphorically when applied to objections against definitions, and whether it can truly apply to transmission (naql) of statements and to bare claims (da'wa). The author first states that using the term 'mamnū' ' (the objected) for questions raised against definitions is metaphorical, though it may be literal if the term is also set for those contexts. Then, in the third investigation (al-bahth al-thālith), he explains that man'—defined as demanding evidence for a specific premise—does not truly apply to transmission or a claim. For transmission, if someone quotes an authority like Abu Hanifa, the objector might deny the attribution or the ruling. Denying the attribution is not a real man' because it is a request to verify the transmission, not a demand for evidence on a premise. The word is used metaphorically because both involve a request. For a claim (e.g., 'the body is composed of indivisible parts'), the objector's 'we do not accept' cannot be a demand for a specific premise (since no evidence has been presented), so it is either meaningless or a request for evidence for the claim itself, which again is metaphorical. The same applies to naqd (counterexample) and mu'āraḍa (counterargument): they do not apply to transmission or claims because there is no established evidence to oppose. The passage ends with a report that some say the only real objection is to the transmitted statement insofar as it is transmitted, due to lack of commitment to its correctness. The commentary clarifies that man' is always a request for evidence on a premise; thus, transmission and claims, lacking a structured argument, cannot be subjected to real man'.

Key Terms
منع (man'): A request for evidence on a specific premise in an argument; an objection aimed at a particular premise. [Note: In this text, man' is narrowly defined as a demand for evidence on a premise, distinguishing it from naqd and mu'āraḍa, whereas in broader usage it can refer to any objection.]
نقل (naql): Transmission of a statement or ruling from an authority, used in debates to cite a source. [Note: No significant shift.]
دعوى (da'wa): A claim or assertion made without supporting evidence, often the initial proposition in a debate. [Note: In dialectical contexts, da'wa is a bare claim that has not yet been supported by evidence.]
نقض (naqd): A counterexample that invalidates a universal claim; a refutation by showing a contradictory instance. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
معارضة (mu'āraḍa): A counterargument that opposes the original evidence by presenting a conflicting argument of equal weight. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the term 'man' ' apply metaphorically when objecting to a transmitted statement?
A) Because the objector is demanding evidence for a premise, which is the real meaning of man'.
B) Because the objector is requesting verification of the transmission, which is a request like man' but not for a premise in an argument.
C) Because the transmitter has already provided evidence for his statement.
D) Because the objection is a false accusation, so it cannot be real.

Key Takeaway
Man' is only literally applicable when objecting to a specific premise in an established argument; against transmissions and bare claims it is used metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate: Speaker A claims 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram).' Opponent B says 'We do not accept that claim.' Is this a real man'? Explain based on the passage.
?
In a definition debate, one objects to the definition of 'salah' as 'specific actions and sayings' by saying 'We do not accept this definition.' According to the commentary, is this man' literal or metaphorical? Why?

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
Real vs. Metaphorical Objection in Dialectic
Objection applies only metaphorically to transmission and claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and metaphorical objection (man') in dialectical debates based on whether the target is a premise, a transmitted statement, or a bare claim.

Core Idea
Objection is truly directed at premises, not at transmitted reports or unbacked claims.

In dialectical debates (jadal), the term 'objection' (man') is frequently used, but not all uses are equal. This commentary clarifies a crucial subtlety: when someone objects to a transmitted statement (naql) or a bare claim (da'wa) that has no proof, the objection is not a real dialectical move — it is only metaphorical. Understanding this distinction protects you from misapplying the rules of debate and ensures your objections target the right logical elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Metaphorical Contexts
The passage specifies two cases where objection is only metaphorical: transmission (naql) and claim (da'wa). Locate these in the Arabic text.
Transmission: quoting another's words. Claim: an assertion without proof.
2
Analyze the Transmission Case
The transmitter merely narrates and does not commit to correctness. Why can't a real objection be made? Because there is no premise from the transmitter to challenge; the objection would be a request to verify the attribution.
The example in the passage is about Abu Hanifa's statement on ablution conditions.
3
Analyze the Claim Case
The speaker makes a claim without proof. The opponent says 'We do not accept that.' This cannot be a real objection because there is no proof yet to request a specific premise; it is a request for proof of the entire claim.
The example: 'The body is composed of indivisible parts.'
4
Understand the Metaphor Mechanism
Both cases involve a request (for verification or for proof), which shares the form of objection. Hence the term is used metaphorically, like using a restricted term for another restricted term.
The passage says 'from the category of using a restricted term for another restricted term for its unrestricted meaning.'
5
Apply to an Islamic Debate
In a debate on the permissibility of ijtihad, a debater cites Imam Shafi'i's opinion. The opponent says: 'We do not accept that Imam Shafi'i said that.' According to this passage, this is a metaphorical objection — a request to verify the transmission, not a real objection to a premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالنَّقْضِ والمُعارَضَة . وقيل : إِنَّما المَمْنُوعُ مَنْعُ المَنْقُولِ مِنْ حيثُ هو مَنْقُولٌ ، لعدم التزام صحته وقد ليست بشرط في الوضوء، فإما أن يقول المانع : لا نسلم أنها ليست بشرط فيه وإما أن يقول : لا نسلم أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى قال كذا ، فالأول لا يسمع أصلا، لأنه قرر الكلام بطريق الحكاية، فلا يتعلق به المؤاخذة أصلا . وأما الثاني فهو - وإن كان يسمع - لكن لا من حيث إنه منع حقيقة ، بل لأنه عبارة عن طلب تصحيح النقل يطلق عليه لفظ المنع مجازا للمشاركة في كون كل منهما طلبا من قبيل استعمال اللفظ المقيد في مقيد آخر لمطلقه ، فاستعمل لفظ المنع . وأما الدعوى فلأنه إذا قال المتكلم : الجسم مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، : ويقول الحكيم : لا نسلم ذلك ، فإما أن يريد به طلب الدليل على المقدمة المعينة ، وهذا مما لا معنى له ، لأنه لم يوجد دليل مع المدعى بعد حتى يطلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة منه ، وإما أن يريد به طلب الدليل على تلك الدعوى ، وهو مسموع ، لكنه ليس بمنع حقيقي، بل إنما يطلق عليه لفظ المنع مجازا على ما عرفت كالنقض والمعارضة ) أي كما أنه لا يتوجه النقض والمعارضة لعدم الدليل المذكور للإثبات . وقيل إنما الممنوع منع المنقول من حيث هو منقول ، لعدم التزام صحته . (1) لما كان المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة معينة من الأدلة التي يقيمها المعلل على دعواه قال المصنف . إن المنع لا يتوجه حقيقة على النقل والدعوى وإنما يتوجه عليهما مجازا . أما النقل فإن الناقل إنما ينقل كلام غيره ولا يتحمل صححه أو فساده فلا يقيم على المنقول أدلة . وإنما يمكن فقط أن يقول السائل . إننا لا نسلم أن يكون ذلك القول لمن نسبته إليه فيكون هذا منعا مجازا لا حقيقة . وأما الدعوى فإنها لم تشتمل على دليل حتى يصح أن يقال أطلب الدليل عليها . لكن يجوز أن يحمل هذا المنع على أن المراد منه إقامة الدليل على الدعوى وعلى ذلك يكون إطلاق المنع على مثل هذا مجازا لا حقيقة . وكذلك لا يتجه النقض والمعارضة على النقل والدعوى كما لا يتجه المنع عليهما .
Like rebuttal (naqd) and counter-argument (mu'arada). And it was said: Indeed, what is prevented is the objection to the transmitted statement (manqul) insofar as it is transmitted, due to the lack of commitment to its correctness. And it has already been said: It is not a condition in ablution. So either the objector says: 'We do not accept that it is not a condition therein' or he says: 'We do not accept that Abu Hanifa - may God have mercy on him - said such.' The first is not heard at all, because he presented the speech in a narrative manner, so no reproach attaches to him at all. As for the second, even if it is heard, it is not insofar as it is a true objection, but because it is a request for correction of the transmission; the term 'objection' is applied metaphorically due to the commonality in that each is a request, from the category of using a restricted term for another restricted term for its unrestricted meaning, so the word 'objection' was used. As for the claim (da'wa), if the speaker says: 'The body is composed of indivisible parts,' and the philosopher says: 'We do not accept that,' then either he intends thereby a request for proof for the specific premise, and this has no meaning because there is no proof with the claimant yet to request a proof for a specific premise from it, or he intends a request for proof for that claim, and this is heard, but it is not a true objection; rather, the word 'objection' is applied to it metaphorically, as you have understood, like rebuttal and counter-argument. (Meaning: just as rebuttal and counter-argument are not directed due to the lack of the mentioned proof for substantiation.) And it was said: Indeed, what is prevented is the objection to the transmitted statement insofar as it is transmitted, due to the lack of commitment to its correctness. (1) Since objection is a request for proof for a specific premise from the proofs that the arguer presents for his claim, the author said: Objection is not directed truly at transmission or claim; rather, it is directed at them metaphorically. As for transmission, the transmitter merely transmits the speech of another and does not bear its correctness or corruption, so he does not present proofs for the transmitted statement. The questioner can only say: 'We do not accept that this statement belongs to the one to whom it is attributed,' and this is a metaphorical objection, not a real one. As for the claim, it does not contain a proof such that it would be correct to say 'I request a proof for it.' However, it is permissible to interpret this objection as intending to establish proof for the claim, and accordingly, applying the term 'objection' to such is metaphorical, not real. Likewise, rebuttal and counter-argument are not directed at transmission or claim, just as objection is not directed at them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining the proper application of 'objection' (man') in dialectical debate (jadal). It clarifies that objection cannot be genuinely leveled against a transmitted statement (naql) or a bare claim (da'wa) that lacks proof; it can only be applied metaphorically. The author begins by linking objection to rebuttal (naqd) and counter-argument (mu'arada). He then presents the view that the prevented objection is to the transmitted statement as such, due to the lack of commitment to its correctness. An example is given from fiqh: someone might object by saying 'We do not accept that Abu Hanifa said such,' which is not a true objection because the transmitter is merely narrating and does not bear the burden of proof. Such an objection is actually a request for verification of the transmission, so the term 'objection' is used metaphorically. Similarly, for a claim like 'The body is composed of indivisible parts,' the opponent's statement 'We do not accept that' cannot be a real objection because there is no proof yet to challenge; it can only be a request for proof of the claim itself, which is metaphorically called objection. The footnote elaborates that objection is defined as a request for proof for a specific premise, but in transmission and claim, there are no premises to challenge, so the usage is metaphorical. This guards against the misunderstanding that one can object to any statement regardless of its type.

Key Terms
منع
نقل
دعوى
التزام الصحة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when someone objects to a transmitted statement (naql) from a scholar, what is the correct understanding?
A) The objection is always valid because the transmitter is responsible for the truth.
B) The objection is metaphorical because the transmitter is merely narrating and does not bear the burden of proof.
C) The objection is a request for the topic of the transmitted statement itself.
D) The objection is invalid because transmitted statements cannot be challenged.

Key Takeaway
Objection (man') in dialectic is truly directed only at premises; it applies only metaphorically to transmitted statements and bare claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a real objection or metaphorical: (a) In a debate about the definition of 'ilm, the opponent says 'We do not accept that al-Ghazali defined it as such.' (b) In a debate about the world's temporality, the opponent says 'We do not accept that the world is composed of indivisible parts' when the speaker has presented no proof.
?
Construct a dialectical exchange where a real objection is made to a premise, and then reconstruct that same objection as metaphorical by converting it into an objection against a transmission.
